Three Grounds for Impugning a Narrator
Author enumerates three ways to object to a hadith by impugning the narrator's integrity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three grounds for objecting to a narrator's integrity cited in the passage and explain why each invalidates the narration.

Core Idea
A narrator is discredited through lying, innovation, or frequent error.

In juristic disputation (jadal), one common strategy to refute a hadith-based argument is to attack the narrator's credibility. The passage provides three specific types of such objections, each illustrated with a concrete example from the hadith corpus. This lesson unpacks those three grounds and shows how they undermine a narration's reliability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ground: lying and fabrication
The passage states that a narrator may be called a 'kadhdhāb yaḍa‘ al-ḥadīth' (liar who fabricates hadith). This is the most severe criticism; it renders the narrator's entire narration invalid. Example: Ibrahim ibn Isma'il and Baraka al-Halabi are cited as such.
2
Identify the second ground: religious innovation (bid'a)
If a narrator is an innovator (mubtadi'), especially one who propagates his innovation (e.g., Qadariyya), his narration is rejected. Example: Mu'bad al-Juhani, a Qadari, whose hadith on laughter in prayer is dismissed.
Not all bid'a is treated equally; here it implies major misguidance.
3
Identify the third ground: frequent error and heedlessness
A narrator known for kathrat al-khaṭā’ wa al-ghafla (frequent error and carelessness) is considered unreliable. Example: Ahmad ibn 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Wahb, whose memory deteriorated in old age.
This corresponds to later terms like 'sū’ al-ḥifẓ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعترض عليه المالكي ويقول : هذا الحديث يرويه إبراهيم بن إسماعيل بن أبي حبيبة المدني، وقد قال جماعة من أصحاب الحديث : «هو كذاب يضع الحديث» ومثله كذلك أن يستدل بما رواه بركة بن محمد الحلبي عن يوسف بن أسباط عن سفيان الثوري عن خالد الحذاء عن ابن سيرين عن أبي هريرة، أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم جعل المضمضة والاستنشاق للجنب ثلاثا ،فريضة، مسنونتان في الوضوء حيث قال أصحاب الحديث بركة الحلبي كذاب يضع الحديث . ثانيها : أن يذكر طعنًا في دينه يوجب بدعته، فيوجب ذلك رد حديثه ؛ مثل أن يستدل أصحاب أبي حنيفة في مسألة القهقهة في الصلاة بما روى معبد الجهني أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أمر من قهقه خلفه بإعادة الوضوء والصلاة فيعترضه المالكي بأن يقول : هذا الحديث يرويه معبد الجهني، وهو قدري داعية، فلا يصح الاحتجاج به. ثالثها: أن يذكر أنه معروف بكثرة الخطأ والغفلة مثل أن يستدل الحنفي في وجوب الوتر، بما روي عن النبي الله أنه قال : (إن الله زادكم صلاة إلى صلواتكم وهي الوتر). فالمخرج بالنسبة للمستدل أمام الاعتراض في هذه الحالات الثلاثة أن يبين للحديث طريقا آخر إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا
The Maliki objects and says: This hadith is narrated by Ibrahim ibn Isma'il ibn Abi Habiba al-Madani, and a group of hadith scholars said: 'He is a liar, fabricates hadith.' And similarly, one may argue using what was narrated by Baraka ibn Muhammad al-Halabi from Yusuf ibn Asbat from Sufyan al-Thawri from Khalid al-Hadhdha' from Ibn Sirin from Abu Hurayra, that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم made rinsing the mouth and snuffing water three times for the junub obligatory, while they are two Sunnahs in wudu, where the hadith scholars said: Baraka al-Halabi is a liar who fabricates hadith. Secondly: that he mentions a criticism of his religion that necessitates his innovation, thus necessitating rejection of his hadith; such as when the followers of Abu Hanifa in the issue of loud laughter in prayer use what Mu'bad al-Juhani narrated that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم ordered whoever laughed loudly behind him to repeat wudu and prayer. The Maliki objects by saying: This hadith is narrated by Mu'bad al-Juhani, and he is a Qadari propagandist, so it is not valid to use it as proof. Thirdly: that he mentions that he is known for frequent error and heedlessness, such as when the Hanafi argues for the obligation of witr using what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'Indeed Allah has added a prayer to your prayers, and it is witr.' So the way out for the arguer in the face of objection in these three cases is to show another chain for the hadith if he can find a way to that.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical illustration of how to object to a hadith by attacking the credibility of its narrator (jarh al-rawi). The author, in the context of jadal (disputation among jurists), presents three specific grounds for such objections. First, the narrator is accused of being a liar and fabricator (kadhab yada' al-hadith), with examples of Ibrahim ibn Isma'il and Baraka al-Halabi. Second, the narrator is accused of religious innovation (bid'a) that calls his reliability into question, exemplified by Mu'bad al-Juhani who was a Qadari (fatalist). Third, the narrator is known for frequent error and heedlessness (kathrat al-khata' wa al-ghafla), as in the case of Ahmad ibn 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Wahb whose memory deteriorated. The author then provides the standard defensive move (makhraj) for the original arguer: to find an alternative chain (tariq akhar) for the same hadith, thereby circumventing the objection. This reflects the usul al-fiqh principle that a hadith's authenticity can be established through multiple routes, even if one chain is weak.

Key Terms
كذاب يضع الحديث
بدعة
كثرة الخطأ والغفلة
المخرج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three grounds for objecting to a narrator's integrity mentioned in the passage?
A) The narrator is a liar who fabricates hadith.
B) The narrator is an innovator (mubtadi') in religion.
C) The narrator is known for frequent error and heedlessness.
D) The narrator is unknown (majhul) in his state.

Key Takeaway
A hadith can be rejected if its narrator is proven to be a liar, an innovator, or frequently mistaken.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Identify which ground applies: A narrator's memory deteriorated in his old age, leading to many errors in his hadith. (Answer: frequent error and heedlessness)

The Makhraj: Rebuttal via Alternative Chains
Author provides the standard rebuttal strategy for narrator integrity objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the makhraj (way out) strategy for rebutting objections to a narrator's integrity and apply it to a new case.

Core Idea
The arguer can save his hadith by producing a second trustworthy chain.

After presenting three types of objections, the author provides the standard defensive move: find another chain for the same hadith. This lesson focuses on that makhraj and its logical role in disputation. Understanding this strategy is crucial for anyone engaging in juristic debate or hadith evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and its force
The Maliki raises an objection that impugns the narrator's integrity (lying, innovation, or frequent error). This objection, if valid, makes the hadith unreliable and defeats the Hanafi's argument.
The objection targets the chain (isnad), not the text (matn).
2
Identify the makhraj: showing an alternative chain
The passage states: 'al-makhraj ... an yubayyin li-l-ḥadīth ṭarīqan ākhar' (the way out is to show another path for the hadith). This means the arguer must find a different transmission route for the same hadith that does not include the criticized narrator.
The alternative chain must be authentic; otherwise the objection persists.
3
Evaluate the effectiveness of the makhraj
If the arguer succeeds in producing a sound alternative chain, the hadith is rehabilitated and the objection is neutralized. If not, the objection stands and the arguer loses that point of debate.
In hadith criticism, this is similar to finding mutaba'at or shawahid.

Rebutting the Jahl Objection in Hadith Criticism
Author explains how to object to a hadith by claiming the narrator is unknown (majhul) and how to rebut by clarifying his status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rebut an objection based on narrator ignorance (jahl) by identifying the two valid rebuttal strategies: alternative chain or clarifying the narrator's state.

Core Idea
Ignorance of a narrator's state can be cured by showing trustworthy scholars narrated from him or praised him.

In usul al-fiqh debates, a hadith can be challenged if a narrator is unknown (majhul). This lesson uses a real Hanafi-Maliki dispute over wudu with nabidh to show how the Hanafi can counter such an objection. You will learn the two methods to remove narrator ignorance and thus salvage the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find where the Maliki objects that the narrator Abu Zayd is unknown (majhul), making the hadith invalid. Note the specific claim.
The objection targets the narrator's unknown status, not the content.
2
Extract the rebuttal strategies
The author gives two ways to rebut: (1) show the hadith has another chain, or (2) remove ignorance by clarifying the narrator's state.
The second strategy is further qualified.
3
Analyze the second strategy
Clarifying the state includes mentioning that trustworthy narrators narrated from him (riwāyat al-thiqāt 'anh) or that hadith scholars praised him (thunā' aṣḥāb al-ḥadīth 'alayh).
This directly addresses the unknown status.
4
Apply to an incomplete example
The text starts another example with Khaled ibn Abi al-Salt. Consider how this example illustrates the method of removing ignorance by showing known narrators transmitting from the allegedly unknown narrator.
The example is cut off but the logical structure is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - الطعن في الراوي من جهة الجهل بحاله : ومثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي على جواز الوضوء بالنبيذ بما روي عن ابن مسعود أنه قال : كنت مع رسول الله لا ليلة الجن، فقال: (أمعك ماء يا ابن مسعود؟) فقلت : لا والله يا رسول الله إلا إداوة فيها نبيذ فقال : (تمرة طيبة وماء طهور). فيعارضه المالكي بأن يقول : هذا الحديث يرويه أبو زيد، وهو مجهول، فلا يصح الاحتجاج به والطريق للتخلص من هذا الاعتراض واستقامة الاحتجاج بالحديث أن يبين المستدل أن للحديث طريقا آخر أو يزيل جهالة الراوي ببيان حاله وذكر رواية الثقات عنه أو ثناء أصحاب الحديث عليه ومثال ذلك ؛ ما روى خالد بن أبي الصلت عن عراك بن مالك عن عائشة أنها
2 - Objection to the narrator from the aspect of ignorance of his state: Its example is that the Hanafi argues for the permissibility of wudu with nabidh using what was narrated from Ibn Mas'ud that he said: I was with the Messenger of Allah on the night of the jinn, and he said: 'Do you have water with you, O Ibn Mas'ud?' I said: No, by Allah, O Messenger of Allah, except a small waterskin with nabidh. He said: 'A good date and pure water.' The Maliki opposes him by saying: This hadith is narrated by Abu Zayd, and he is unknown, so it is not valid to use it as proof. The way to avoid this objection and to establish proof using the hadith is that the arguer shows that the hadith has another chain or removes the ignorance of the narrator by clarifying his state, mentioning the narration of trustworthy narrators from him or the praise of hadith scholars upon him. And an example of that is what Khaled ibn Abi al-Salt narrated from 'Irak ibn Malik from 'A'isha that she...
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical discussion of objecting to a hadith through narrator criticism, now focusing on a second category: ignorance of the narrator's state (jahl bi hālih). The author gives a concrete example: the Hanafi uses a hadith narrated by Abu Zayd (who is unknown) to justify wudu with nabidh (date water). The Maliki objects that Abu Zayd is majhūl (unknown), making the hadith unacceptable. The author then presents the defensive strategy (ṭarīq) for the arguer: either find another chain for the same hadith, or remove the ignorance about the narrator by providing information about his state—such as showing that trustworthy scholars narrated from him (riwāyat al-thiqāt 'anh) or that hadith scholars praised him (thunā' aṣḥāb al-ḥadīth 'alayh). The text then gives an incomplete example of Khaled ibn Abi al-Salt narrating from 'Irak ibn Malik from 'A'isha, likely to illustrate the method of removing ignorance by showing that known scholars transmitted from the allegedly unknown narrator. This reflects the usul al-fiqh principle that a narrator's unknown status can be remedied if his reliability is established through external evidence.

Objection to a hadith due to weak narrator and way out
Maliki objects to a hadith based on narrator's memory deterioration; answer suggests finding another chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grounds of a narrator-based objection in jadal and formulate the appropriate makhraj (way out) of finding an alternative chain.

Core Idea
A narrator's memory failure can break a hadith, but a stronger chain can save it.

In jadal, when an opponent attacks a hadith by impugning a narrator's memory, the one adducing the proof cannot simply ignore the objection. The classical method, as shown by al-Baji, is to seek another chain of transmission for the same hadith. This lesson dissects such an objection and its standard response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (naqd)
Locate the Maliki's claim: the hadith is narrated by Ahmad ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Wahb, whose memory had deteriorated (taghayyara hifzuhu wa saa'a). This renders the hadith unreliable.
The objection targets the narrator's credibility, not the content.
2
Analyze the grounds: memory deterioration and mixing
The opponent specifies that Ahmad's memory changed and worsened, leading to ikhtilat (mixing of narrations). This is a recognized form of narrator weakness in hadith criticism.
ikhtilat is a technical term for confusion in later life.
3
Determine the effect on the proof
Because of the narrator's weakness, the hadith 'is not valid to rely on' (fala yasihhu al-ihtijaj bihi). The objection succeeds in removing this hadith as evidence.
The opponent has effectively blocked this particular chain.
4
Extract the makhraj (way out)
The text states the solution: 'the hadith has another chain' (li-l-hadith tariqun akhar). The proponent should locate an alternative chain that is not tainted by the weak narrator.
This is the first of three possible responses listed later in the source.
5
Evaluate the dialectical move
Finding another chain does not refute the objection; it bypasses it by providing stronger evidence. This is a valid jadal strategy because it shifts the basis of proof.
If no alternative chain exists, the proponent must resort to other responses (e.g., arguing that the narrator's weakness is not decisive).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول المالكي : هذا الحديث يرويه أحمد بن عبد الرحمن بن وهب عن عمه، وكان تغير حفظه وساء، وهذا الحديث من جملة ما أنكر عليه وعرف به اختلاطه فلا يصح الاحتجاج به . والمخرج بالنسبة للمستدل أمام الاعتراض في هذه الحالات الثلاثة أن للحديث طريقا آخر إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا (1) (1) المنهاج في ترتيب الحجاج، للباجي، ص: 79
The Maliki says: This hadith is narrated by Ahmad ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Wahb from his uncle, and his memory had changed and deteriorated, and this hadith is among what was rejected from him and it is known that his mixing occurred, so it is not valid to rely on it. And the way out for the one adducing the proof in the face of the objection in these three cases is that the hadith has another chain if he finds a way to that. (1) (1) Al-Minhaj fi Tartib al-Hijaj, by al-Baji, p. 79.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection (naqd) to a hadith used as evidence. The opponent (Maliki) claims the narrator Ahmad ibn Abd al-Rahman ibn Wahb had memory deterioration and mixing of narrations, rendering the hadith unreliable. The answer for the one adducing the proof is to find another chain of transmission for the same hadith, if available. This is the first of three types of objections and their general solution explained later.

Key Terms
تغير حفظه وساء
اختلاط
المخرج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a narrator-based objection can be answered by producing an alternative chain, preserving the proof's strength.

Objection to a Hadith as Mursal: Definition and Dialectical Use
Definition of mursal hadith and its use as an objection in jadal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of mursal hadith according to both muhaddithun and usuliyyun, and explain how it is used as an objection in jadal.

Core Idea
The mursal hadith is a dialectical tool: its definition and acceptance vary among schools.

This passage introduces the third type of objection in usuli jadal: challenging a hadith because it is mursal. We will examine the two definitions of mursal, the scholarly disagreement on its validity, and see how such an objection is raised in practice. Understanding this helps you recognise a key move in juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of mursal
Note that the text gives two definitions: according to hadith scholars (muhaddithun), it is when a tabi'i omits the companion and says directly 'The Prophet said'; according to usuliyyun, it is the statement of a just person ('adl) who did not meet the Prophet, saying 'The Prophet said'.
The usuliyyun definition adds the condition of 'adalah (justness).
2
Recognise the scholarly disagreement
The text states that al-Shafi'i rejected acting upon mursal, while Abu Hanifa, Malik, and the majority of Mu'tazila accepted it. Note that acceptance is not universal.
This disagreement makes mursal a potent point of objection.
3
Examine the example of objection
The Maliki uses a hadith as evidence. The opponent objects that the hadith is mursal. The footnote shows the hadith was narrated by Ibn Majah, al-Daraqutni, and Ahmad. In a debate, the objector would highlight that the chain lacks a companion.
The objection is valid only if the opponent's school does not accept mursal, or if the proponent's own school's definition is violated.
4
Understand the dialectical move
The objector must show that the hadith fits the definition of mursal according to the proponent's own usuli definition. The proponent may then defend by citing his school's acceptance of mursal.
This illustrates how jadal proceeds: objections target the chain or acceptance criteria, not just content.

Commentary
This passage describes the third type of objection: the hadith is mursal. Then an example is given: the Maliki uses a certain hadith, and the opponent objects that it is mursal. The footnote provides the source of the hadith. Responding to an Objection of Unknown Narrator
Author gives an example of an objection to a hadith based on the narrator being unknown, and the response defending him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection based on narrator unknown (majhool) and construct a valid response using evidence of the narrator's reliability from reputable scholars.

Core Idea
Claiming a narrator is unknown is not automatically fatal; the defender can rebut by citing those who attest to his reliability.

In dialectical exchanges within usul al-fiqh, opponents often challenge a hadith by asserting that a narrator is majhool (unknown). This passage illustrates a standard rebuttal: the defender brings testimonies from respected scholars that the narrator is indeed known and reliable. Understanding this move equips you to handle similar objections in hadith-based arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial proof
Locate the hadith that the opponent is attacking. Here, it is the narration about facing the qibla in the toilet, reported on the authority of Khalid ibn Abi al-Salt.
The hadith is given first, then the objection follows.
2
Extract the objection and its basis
The opponent explicitly states: 'Khalid ibn Abi al-Salt is unknown (majhool).' This is the core of the objection – attacking the credibility of a narrator to undermine the hadith.
The objection uses the term 'majhool' to claim that the narrator is not identified or reliable.
3
Analyze the response
The defender, Abu Thawr via Abu Bakr ibn al-Mundhir, responds by citing Ahmad ibn Hanbal's positive assessment and naming three reliable narrators who narrated from Khalid, establishing that he is known.
The response does not deny the objection abstractly; it provides concrete evidence of Khalid's fame among trustworthy scholars.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
If the evidence is accepted, the objection is rebutted. The hadith remains valid unless another objection is raised. This shows that a 'majhool' claim is not decisive when countered by testimony of reliability.
The strength of the response depends on the credibility of the cited authorities (Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Khalid al-Hadha', etc.).

فقال المخالف معترضا : خالد بن أبي الصلت مجهول .
(2)
حكى أبو بكر بن المنذر هذا عن أبي ثور(3)، وأجاب عنه فقال : هذا ليس بصحيح، لأن أحمد بن حنبل - رضي الله عنه - قال: «مخرج هذا الحديث حسن» .

And others said: Khalid is known; narrated from him are Khalid al-Hadha', Mubarak ibn Fadala, and Wasil, the client of Abu 'Uyayna, and these are reliable, so it is necessary that Khalid ibn Abi al-Salt is known.

The response uses testimony from Ahmad ibn Hanbal and others to establish Khalid ibn Abi al-Salt's reliability. The structure shows the standard method of countering a 'majhool' objection: provide evidence that the narrator is well-known to reliable scholars.

Objection on Grounds of Mursal: A Dialectical Exchange
Author defines mursal, discusses disagreement, gives jadal example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the jadal exchange between a Maliki and a Shafi'i over a mursal hadith and identify the grounds for accepting or rejecting it.

Core Idea
A mursal hadith is accepted by some schools and rejected by others, making it a valid ground for objection in usul al-fiqh.

In this passage, the author introduces the third type of objection against a hadith: that it is mursal. He clarifies the definition of mursal for both hadith scholars and legal theorists, then notes the disagreement among usuliyyin on its acceptability. Through a concrete example of a Maliki citing a mursal hadith and a Shafi'i objecting, we see how this dialectical move works. This lesson will walk you through the logic of the exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of mursal
Note the two definitions: for muhaddithun, it is a tabi'i omitting the intermediary; for usuliyyun, it is a just person who never met the Prophet narrating directly. Understand the shift.
This definitional difference affects how the objection is framed.
2
Locate the disagreement on acceptance
Find which scholars accept mursal as proof (Abu Hanifa, Malik, Mu'tazila) and which reject it (al-Shafi'i).
This disagreement is the basis for the objection.
3
Examine the example exchange
Read the Maliki argument (using the hadith 'No one touches the Quran except the pure') and the Shafi'i objection that it is mursal and not a proof.
Identify the roles: mudda'i (Maliki) and mu'tarid (Shafi'i).
4
Analyze the Maliki response
Note the two possible responses: show the chain is continuous (muttasil) or assert that mursal is a proof in his school.
The second response is a school-based move, not a textual one.
5
Review later scholarly positions
Learn that al-Baji and Abu Ya'la hold that mursal cannot be used as an objection (since their schools accept it), while al-Shirazi and Ibn 'Aqil allow it.
This shows the objection's validity depends on the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
3 - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالحديث من جهة كونه مرسلا : ومعنى الحديث المرسل عند المحدثين : ترك التابعي الواسطة بينه وبين النبي ، فيقول : قال رسول الله ، أما عند الأصوليين فهو: قول العدل الذي لم يلق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «قال رسول الله . . . ( 5 ) .
وهذا النوع من الأحاديث لم يكن الاستدلال به محل اتفاق بين الأصوليين، فقد رفض الشافعي العمل به، وقال أبو حنيفة ومالك وجمهور المعتزلة : إنه مقبول (6)
و مثال الاعتراض على الاستدلال على الحديث بأنه مرسل ؛ أن يستدل المالكي بعدم جواز مس المصحف إلا من طاهر، بما روى مالك عن عبد الله بن أبي بكر ابن محمد بن عمرو بن حزم ؛ أن في الكتاب الذي كتبه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لعمرو بن حزم : (لا يمس القرآن إلا طاهر) (1) .
فيعترضه شافعي فيقول : هذا حديث مرسل، وليس بحجة . ويجيب المالكي على هذا الاعتراض إما ببيان اتصال السند أو بأن يقول: «إن المرسل حجة، وهذا أصل من أصولنا ونحن نبني فروعنا على أصولنا»(2).
وقد ذهب كل من أبي الوليد الباجي وأبي يعلى الفراء إلى أن الإرسال لا يعترض به على الحديث، لأن الصحيح في مذهبيهما جواز الاحتجاج به، وكذلك يصح أن يعترض عليه به ) ، في حين ذهب أبو إسحاق الشيرازي وأبو الوفاء ابن عقيل ومن يوافقهما في مذهبيهما إلى جواز الاعتراض بالإرسال
3 - Objection to the deduction by the hadith on the basis of it being mursal: The meaning of mursal hadith according to the hadith scholars is: the follower (tabi'i) omits the intermediary between him and the Prophet, so he says: 'The Messenger of Allah said...' Whereas according to the legal theorists (usuliyyin) it is: the statement of a just person who did not meet the Prophet: 'The Messenger of Allah said...' (5) This type of hadith was not agreed upon among the legal theorists as a basis for deduction. Al-Shafi'i rejected acting upon it, while Abu Hanifa, Malik, and the majority of the Mu'tazila said it is acceptable. (6) An example of objecting to the deduction by a hadith on the basis that it is mursal: that a Maliki deduces the impermissibility of touching the Quran except when pure, by what Malik narrated from 'Abdullah ibn Abi Bakr ibn Muhammad ibn 'Amr ibn Hazm: that in the letter that the Messenger of Allah wrote to 'Amr ibn Hazm: 'No one touches the Quran except the pure.' (1) So a Shafi'i objects to him and says: This is a mursal hadith, and it is not a proof. The Maliki responds to this objection either by showing the continuity of the chain or by saying: 'The mursal is a proof, and this is one of our principles, and we build our branches on our principles.' (2) Both Abu al-Walid al-Baji and Abu Ya'la al-Farra' held that mursal is not to be objected against the hadith, because the correct in their two schools is that it is permissible to use it as proof, and likewise it is valid to object with it. While Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi and Abu al-Wafa' Ibn 'Aqil and those who agree with them in their two schools held that it is permissible to object with mursal.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage explains the third type of objection against a hadith: that it is mursal. First, the author defines mursal hadith differently for hadith scholars (muhaddithun) and legal theorists (usuliyyun): for the former, it is a tabi'i omitting the intermediary; for the latter, it is a just person who never met the Prophet narrating directly. He then notes that there is no consensus on accepting mursal as proof: al-Shafi'i rejects it, while Abu Hanifa, Malik, and the Mu'tazila accept it. An example follows: a Maliki argues that touching the Quran requires purity, citing a mursal hadith from Malik. A Shafi'i objects that the hadith is mursal and thus not a proof. The Maliki can respond either by showing that the chain is actually continuous (muttasil) or by asserting that mursal is valid evidence in his school. Finally, the author reports later scholarly opinions: al-Baji and Abu Ya'la hold that mursal cannot be used as an objection because their schools accept it as proof, while al-Shirazi and Ibn 'Aqil hold that it can be used as an objection.

Jadal Example: Objection by Irsal on the Hadith of Touching the Quran
Author gives an example of a jadal exchange and discusses validity of objection by irsal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct a jadal exchange by identifying the claimant's position, the objector's grounds, and the respondent's strategies, using the Maliki-Shafi'i debate on touching the Quran.

Core Idea
In jadal, the validity of an objection depends on the opponent's own principles, not the objector's.

This passage presents a classic jadal exchange from usul al-fiqh. A Maliki cites a mursal hadith to support a ruling; a Shafi'i objects on the grounds that mursal is not authoritative. The Maliki can respond in two ways, revealing a core dialectical principle: the validity of an objection is relative to the opponent's school. This lesson will guide you through dismantling the exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudda'i's position
Find the Maliki's claim: it is impermissible to touch the Quran except by a pure person. The evidence is the hadith from Malik.
2
Find the mu'tarid's objection
The Shafi'i objects that the hadith is mursal (missing a link) and therefore not a valid proof (hujja) in his school.
The objection is based on a flaw in the chain of transmission.
3
Analyse the jawab options
The Maliki can respond either by proving the chain is actually connected (ittiṣāl al-sanad) or by asserting that his school accepts mursal as evidence, making it a valid proof.
The second response appeals to the opponent's own principles, a key jadal move.
4
Assess scholarly disagreement on the objection
Al-Baji and Abu Ya'la hold the objection invalid because the objector's school (Hanbali? Actually al-Baji is Maliki, Abu Ya'la Hanbali) accepts mursal, so the objection would not affect the Maliki. Al-Shirazi and Ibn 'Aqil hold it valid because the proponent may be using a hadith not accepted by his own school.
The disagreement hinges on whose principles are invoked to judge the objection.
5
Derive the dialectical principle
The passage shows that in jadal, an objection based on a flaw in evidence is only effective if the opponent's own school recognizes that flaw. Otherwise, the objection fails to undermine the claim.
This principle is fundamental in usul al-fiqh debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعدم جواز مس المصحف إلا من طاهر، بما روى مالك عن عبد الله بن أبي بكر ابن محمد بن عمرو بن حزم ؛ أن في الكتاب الذي كتبه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لعمرو بن حزم : (لا يمس القرآن إلا طاهر) . فيعترضه شافعي فيقول : هذا حديث مرسل، وليس بحجة . ويجيب المالكي على هذا الاعتراض إما ببيان اتصال السند أو بأن يقول: «إن المرسل حجة، وهذا أصل من أصولنا ونحن نبني فروعنا على أصولنا». وقد ذهب كل من أبي الوليد الباجي وأبي يعلى الفراء إلى أن الإرسال لا يعترض به على الحديث، لأن الصحيح في مذهبيهما جواز الاحتجاج به، وكذلك يصح أن يعترض عليه به، في حين ذهب أبو إسحاق الشيرازي وأبو الوفاء ابن عقيل ومن يوافقهما في مذهبيهما إلى جواز الاعتراض بالإرسال
Concerning the impermissibility of touching the Quran except by a pure person, based on what Malik narrated from Abdullah ibn Abi Bakr ibn Muhammad ibn Amr ibn Hazm: that in the letter that the Messenger of Allah wrote to Amr ibn Hazm: 'None touches the Quran except a pure person.' Then a Shafi'i objects saying: This hadith is mursal and not a proof. And the Maliki responds to this objection either by clarifying the connectedness of the chain or by saying: 'Indeed the mursal is a proof, and this is one of our principles, and we build our derived rulings on our principles.' And each of Abu al-Walid al-Baji and Abu Ya'la al-Farra' went to the view that mursal is not to be objected to on the hadith, because the correct view in their schools is the permissibility of using it as evidence, and likewise it is permissible to object with it, while Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi and Abu al-Wafa ibn 'Aqil and those who agree with them in their schools went to the permissibility of objecting by mursal.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a typical jadal (dialectical) example. It presents a Maliki who cites a hadith to support the ruling that only a pure person can touch the Quran. The hadith is reported by Malik as a mursal (a hadith with a missing link in the chain). A Shafi'i objector points out that the hadith is mursal and therefore not a valid proof. The Maliki has two possible responses: either prove that the chain is actually connected (bi-bayan ittisal al-sanad), or argue that in his school, mursal is accepted as a proof. The passage then notes the positions of different scholars on whether such an objection (i.e., pointing out the mursal status) is valid in jadal. Al-Baji and Abu Ya'la al-Farra' hold that the objection is not valid because if the objector's school accepts mursal, then the objection doesn't stand; indeed, the objector would be bound by the hadith. In contrast, al-Shirazi and Ibn 'Aqil hold that the objection is valid, because the proponent may be using a hadith that his own school does not accept. This example illustrates a core dialectical principle: the validity of an objection depends on the principles of the opponent, not merely on the objector's own criteria.

Key Terms
مرسل (mursal): A hadith with a missing link in its chain of transmission, specifically a report from a Successor who does not mention the Companion. Here it is used as an objection to the validity of the hadith as evidence. [Note: In hadith terminology, mursal is a category of weak hadith; in jadal, it is a common objection against hadith evidence, and its acceptance varies by school.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the jadal example, what is the Shafi'i's main objection to the Maliki's hadith?
A) The hadith is fabricated.
B) The hadith is mursal and not a valid proof in his school.
C) The hadith contradicts the Quran.
D) The hadith is weak due to a missing Companion.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an objection in jadal is relative: it depends on whether the opponent's own school recognizes the flaw cited.

The First Type of Objection: Proponent Rejects His Own Evidence
Classification of objections to the matn with focus on first type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first type of objection to the matn and evaluate Ibn 'Aqil's critique of the standard response.

Core Idea
A proponent cannot validly argue using evidence he himself rejects, even if his opponent accepts it.

In dialectical argument (jadal), objections (i'tiradat) are raised against the evidence presented. The author classifies objections to the original statement (al-qawl al-mubtada') of the Sunnah. The first major type is when the proponent (mustadill) himself does not accept the evidence he is using. This lesson unpacks that objection, the standard answer, and Ibn 'Aqil's powerful refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of the Sunnah and the focus on original statements.
The author divides the Sunnah into qawl (statement), fi'l (action), and iqrar (approval). Qawl is further divided into mubtada' (original) and kharij 'ala sabab (occasioned). The focus is on objections to qawl mubtada'.
This classification frames the subsequent discussion.
2
Locate the first type of objection: proponent does not accept the evidence.
Read the description: 'al-i'tirad min jihat anna al-mustadill la yaqul bi al-dalil.' The example given is Hanafis using khabar al-wahid in matters of 'umum al-balwa or wherever they reject it.
This is a specific sub-type of objection targeting the proponent's own inconsistency.
3
Analyze the standard response to this objection.
The standard answer: 'If I do not accept it, but you accept it, then you are obliged to act upon it.' This response shifts the burden to the opponent.
This response seems plausible but is criticized.
4
Examine Ibn 'Aqil's critique of this response.
Ibn 'Aqil argues that the proponent must argue from his own principles; using evidence he does not accept is not genuine istidlal but a corruption of the opponent's school. The proponent's form is that of a proponent but his meaning is that of a mulzim (one who compels).
This critique establishes a key principle of dialectical ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: الاعتراضات الواردة على الاستدلال بالمتن (القول المبتدأ). متن السنة ثلاثة أقسام: قول وفعل وإقرار، فأما القول فنوعان: مبتدأ، وخارج على سبب. ويهمنا هنا أن نفصل القول في الاعتراضات الواردة على القول المبتدأ، ونخصص لكل من الفعل والإقرار والقول الخارج على سبب مباحث خاصة. فإذن الاعتراضات الواردة على المتن، وهو القول المبتدأ، هي الاعتراضات الواردة على الكتاب، وتفصيلها كما يلي: المطلب الأول: الاعتراض من جهة أن المستدل لا يقول بالدليل وهو على ثلاثة أوجه: 1 - أن يستدل بحديث وهو ممن لا يقبل مثل ذلك الحديث في أصوله: كاستدلال الأحناف بخبر الواحد فيما يعم به البلوى، أو فيما خالفه القياس، وما أشبه ذلك مما لا يقبل فيه بخبر الواحد عندهم. فإذا اعترض على المستدل بهذا، فإن جوابه أن يقول للمعترض: إن كنت أنا لا أقول به إلا أنك أنت تقول به، وهو حجة عندك، فيلزمك العمل به. وقد رد ابن عقيل الاستدلال بمثل هذا ما دام المستدل لا يقول به، وكون مخالفه يقول به لا يعطيه الحق هو في أن يستدل به عليه، يقول: وعندي أنه لا يحسن الاستدلال بمثل هذا لأن الدليل على المذهب المسؤول عنه إنما يكون بما يعتقده المستدل دليلا، فإذا استدل بما يعتقده غيره دليلا وهو يعتقده غير دليل لم يكن مستدلا، لكن صورته صورة المستدل، ومعناه معنى الملتزم على مذهب غيره والمفسد لمذهب غيره، وليس هذا من الاستدلال في شيء.
Second section: Objections that occur on the evidence from the text (the original statement). The text of the Sunnah has three divisions: statement, action, and approval. As for statement, it is of two types: original and occasioned by a cause. Our concern here is to elaborate on the objections occurring on the original statement, and we will dedicate separate sections to each of action, approval, and the occasioned statement. So then the objections occurring on the text, which is the original statement, are the objections occurring on the scripture, and their detail is as follows: First demand: the objection from the aspect that the proponent does not accept the evidence, and it has three aspects: 1 - That he uses a hadith as evidence while he is from those who do not accept such a hadith in his principles: like the Hanafis using a solitary report in matters of general necessity, or in matters that contradict analogical reasoning, and similar things which they do not accept solitary reports for. So if an objection is made against the proponent with this, then his answer is to say to the objector: If I do not accept it, but you accept it, and it is a proof in your view, then you are obliged to act upon it. And Ibn 'Aqil rejected using such an argument as long as the proponent does not accept it, and the fact that his opponent accepts it does not give him the right to use it against him. He said: In my view it is not good to use such an argument because the evidence for the school concerning which he is asked is only by what the proponent considers evidence. If he argues by what someone else considers evidence while he considers it not evidence, then he is not a proponent; his form is the form of a proponent but his meaning is the meaning of one who is committed to the school of another and corrupting the school of another, and this is not from argumentation at all.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a classification of objections to the text (matn) of the Sunnah, specifically focusing on the original statement (al-qawl al-mubtada'). It first divides the Sunnah into three parts: statements, actions, and approvals. Statements are further divided into original and occasioned. The focus here is on the original statement. The first type of objection (al-matalib al-awwal) is when the proponent himself does not accept the evidence he is using. Three sub-aspects are possible, but only the first is detailed: the proponent uses a hadith that his own school does not accept. The example given is the Hanafis using a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) in matters of general necessity (ma 'amma bihi al-balwa) or in matters that contradict analogical reasoning (qiyas)—areas where the Hanafi school does not accept solitary reports. The standard response to this objection is to turn it back on the objector: 'I may not accept it, but you do, so you are obliged by it.' However, Ibn 'Aqil strongly criticizes this response. He argues that proper argumentation requires that the proponent use evidence that he himself believes in. If he uses evidence that only his opponent believes, he is not genuinely arguing for his own position; he is merely acting as if he were committed to the opponent's school, which is a corruption of dialectic. Thus, Ibn 'Aqil holds that such an argument is invalid and not true istidlal. This passage teaches that dialectical argument must be based on shared or at least accepted principles by the proponent, not merely on the opponent's acceptance.

Key Terms
المستدل
خبر الواحد
الاعتراض

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Aqil, why is it not valid for a proponent to use an evidence that he himself does not accept?
A) Because the opponent will not accept it.
B) Because evidence must be accepted by both parties.
C) Because the proponent must argue based on his own principles.
D) Because solitary reports are never accepted.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a proponent must base his argument on evidence he himself considers valid; using evidence from the opponent's school alone does not constitute proper istidlal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a scenario where a Maliki debater uses a hadith that his school does not accept in matters of 'umum al-balwa (similar to the Hanafi example), identify the type of objection and explain whether Ibn 'Aqil would consider this valid argumentation.

Objection: Proponent Does Not Accept His Own Evidence
Author classifies three sub-aspects of objection that the proponent does not adhere to his own evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the dialectical objection that the proponent does not accept his own evidence, and apply Ibn 'Aqil's criterion for valid inference in usuli debates.

Core Idea
A proponent cannot cite evidence he does not himself believe—otherwise he is not inferring but imposing.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), objections are not always about the truth or authenticity of the evidence. Sometimes the objection targets the proponent's own commitment to the evidence he presents. This passage from a work on jadal in usul al-fiqh classifies three ways this objection arises, with examples from Hanafi-Shaifi debates and a critical ruling from Ibn 'Aqil. Understanding these distinctions will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments in Islamic legal and theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
The objection is not about the evidence being weak or false, but about the proponent (mustadill) not accepting it in his own school. Recognize that this is a dialectical move that questions the proponent's consistency.
The key phrase is 'la yaqulu bi al-dalil' – he does not accept the evidence.
2
Analyze the first sub-aspect
The proponent cites a type of hadith (e.g., khabar al-wahid) that his own school rejects in certain contexts (like general applicability or contradicting qiyas). Notice how the objection targets the type of evidence, not the specific narration.
Example: Hanafi cites khabar al-wahid in matters generally known or contradicting analogy.
3
Examine the proponent's answer and Ibn 'Aqil's critique
The proponent can say: 'You (the objector) accept it, so it's binding on you.' But Ibn 'Aqil rejects this: the proponent must genuinely believe the evidence for it to be a valid inference. Otherwise, it's just a corrupting tactic.
Ibn 'Aqil's criterion: 'The evidence for the school asked about is only what the proponent believes to be evidence.'
4
Analyze the second sub-aspect
The proponent uses a method of inference his school does not accept, such as dalil al-khitab (implication of the address). The objector points this out. The proponent may answer that some scholars in his school accept it, or that the wording indicates a limit (ghaya) which he does accept.
Examples: Hanafi uses 'in sa'imati al-ghanam al-zakat' to infer no zakat on fed sheep; or the hadith on selling food before possession to infer option of session.
5
Reflect on the third sub-aspect (incomplete)
The passage begins to discuss a third aspect: the proponent does not accept the evidence in the specific context it is reported. The text is cut off, but the example involves a Hanafi citing a hadith about a free man killed for a slave.
This aspect would likely involve disagreement over the interpretation or application of a hadith in a particular case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني، الاعتراضات الواردة على الاستدلال بالمتن القول المبتدأ) متن السنة ثلاثة أقسام قول وفعل وإقرار، فأما القول فنوعان : مبتدأ، وخارج على سبب . ويهمنا هنا أن نفصل القول في الاعتراضات الواردة على القول المبتدأ، ونخصص لكل من الفعل والإقرار والقول الخارج على سبب مباحث خاصة. فإذن الاعتراضات الواردة على المتن، وهو القول المبتدأ، هي الاعتراضات الواردة على الكتاب، وتفصيلها كما يلى :
المطلب الأول : الاعتراض من جهة أن المستدل لا يقول بالدليل وهو على ثلاثة أوجه :
1 - أن يستدل بحديث وهو ممن لا يقبل مثل ذلك الحديث في أصوله : کاستدلال الأحناف بخبر الواحد فيما يعم به البلوى، أو فيما خالفه القياس، وما أشبه ذلك مما لا يقبل فيه بخبر الواحد عندهم فإذا اعترض على المستدل بهذا، فإن جوابه أن يقول للمعترض: إن كنت أنا لا أقول به إلا أنك أنت تقول به، وهو حجة عندك، فيلزمك العمل به وقد رد ابن عقيل الاستدلال بمثل هذا ما دام المستدل لا يقول به، وكون مخالفه يقول به لا يعطيه الحق هو في أن يستدل به عليه، يقول: وعندي أنه لا يحسن الاستدلال بمثل هذا لأن الدليل على المذهب المسؤول عنه إنما يكون بما يعتقده المستدل دليلا، فإذا استدل بما يعتقده غيره دليلا وهو يعتقده غير دليل لم يكن مستدلا، لكن صورته صورة المستدل، ومعناه معنى الملتزم على مذهب غيره والمفسد لمذهب غيره، وليس هذا من الاستدلال في شيء.
2 - أن يستدل فيه بطريق لا يقول به :
وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنفي في أنه لا زكاة في الغنم المعلوفة بما روي عن النبي أنه قال : ( في سائمة الغنم الزكاة )، وهذا يدل على انتفاء الزكاة عن المعلوفة . أو يستدل في إبطال خيار المجلس، بما روي عن النبي (أنه نهى عن بيع الطعام حتى يقبض) ، فدل على أنه إذا قبض في المجلس جاز بيعه . فيعترض عليه المالكي أو الحنبلي، بأن يقول : لا يصح احتجاجك بهذا الخبر، لأن وجه الدليل منه لا تقول به، وذلك أن هذا استدلال بدليل الخطاب أو مفهوم المخالفة، وأنت لا تقول به، وقد يجيب الحنفي على هذا الاعتراض بأن هذه طريقة لبعض أصحابنا وأنا ممن يقول به (3) . . أو يقول، كما في الحديث الثاني : إن هذا اللفظ للغاية، وأنا أقول به فيما علق الحكم فيه على الغاية(4)
3 - أن لا يقول به في الموضع الذي ورد فيه :
وذلك كاستدلال الحنفي على أن الحر يقتل بالعبد، بقول النبي عليه الصلاة
The second topic: Objections raised against inference from the text, the initial statement. The text of the Sunnah is three divisions: statement, action, and tacit approval. As for the statement, it is two types: initial and arising from a cause. What concerns us here is to detail the discussion of objections against the initial statement, and we will dedicate separate sections to each of action, tacit approval, and the statement arising from a cause. So, the objections against the text, which is the initial statement, are the objections against the book, and their detail is as follows: The first request: The objection from the angle that the proponent does not accept the evidence, and it has three aspects: 1 - That he cites a hadith while he is one who does not accept such a hadith in his principles: like the Hanafis citing a khabar al-wahid in matters that are generally applicable, or in matters that contradict analogy, and the like, where they do not accept khabar al-wahid. So if the proponent is objected to with this, then his answer is to say to the objector: 'If I do not accept it, you do accept it, and it is a proof according to you, so you are obligated to act upon it.' Ibn 'Aqil has rejected citing such as long as the proponent does not accept it, and that his opponent's acceptance does not give him the right to cite it against him. He says: In my view, it is not proper to cite such a thing, because the evidence for the school asked about is only what the proponent believes to be evidence. So if he cites what someone else believes to be evidence while he himself does not believe it to be evidence, he is not a proponent; rather, his form is the form of a proponent, but his meaning is the meaning of one who imposes another's school and corrupts another's school, and this is nothing like inference. 2 - That he cites by a method he does not accept: Such as a Hanafi citing that there is no zakat on fed sheep based on what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'On grazing sheep there is zakat', and this indicates the absence of zakat on fed sheep. Or he cites in invalidating the option of the session, based on what was narrated from the Prophet that he forbade selling food until it is taken possession, so it indicates that if he takes possession in the session, its sale is permissible. Then a Maliki or Hanbali objects by saying: Your argument with this report is not valid, because the aspect of the evidence from it you do not accept, and that is because this is an inference from daleel al-khitab or concept of opposition, and you do not accept that. And the Hanafi may answer this objection by saying: This is a method of some of our companions, and I am one who accepts it. Or he says, as in the second hadith: This wording is for the limit, and I accept it regarding what the ruling is attached to the limit. 3 - That he does not accept it in the specific context it is reported in: Such as a Hanafi citing that a free man is killed for a slave, based on the Prophet's saying, peace be upon him...
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is from a book on dialectic (jadal) in usul al-fiqh. It introduces the second major topic: objections against inference from the text (al-matan), specifically the 'initial statement' (al-qawl al-mubtada'). The author first categorizes the Sunna into three types: statement, action, and tacit approval. Statements are of two types: initial and arising from a cause. The focus here is on objections against the initial statement. The passage then details the first type of objection: that the proponent (mustadill) himself does not accept the evidence he cites. This is subdivided into three aspects. The first aspect is when the proponent cites a hadith whose type he normally does not accept, e.g., a Hanafi citing a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) in a matter that is widely applicable or contradicts analogy, since the Hanafi school generally does not accept such reports in those contexts. The author provides the answer: the proponent can say to the objector, 'If I do not accept it, you do accept it, and it is binding on you.' However, Ibn 'Aqil rejects this, arguing that the proponent must genuinely believe in the evidence for it to be valid inference; otherwise he is merely imposing his opponent's view and corrupting it, not truly inferring. The second aspect is when the proponent uses a method of inference he does not accept, e.g., a Hanafi using daleel al-khitab (implication of the address) or concept of opposition (mafhum al-mukhalafa) which he generally rejects. The author gives examples from Hanafi fiqh: citing the hadith 'on grazing sheep there is zakat' to prove no zakat on fed sheep (by implication of the limit), and citing the prohibition of selling food until taken possession to prove that if taken possession in the session it is permissible (again by implication). The objector (Maliki or Hanbali) points out that this method is not accepted by the Hanafi. The Hanafi may answer by saying he follows some Hanafi scholars who do accept it, or he may argue that the wording indicates a limit (ghaya) which he accepts. The third aspect is cut off in the text, but it concerns the proponent not accepting the evidence in the specific context it is reported in; the example given is a Hanafi citing a hadith about a free man being killed for a slave, but the passage ends. This passage illustrates the dialectical principle that the validity of an argument depends on the proponent's own acceptance of the premises and methods; otherwise the objection is valid.

Key Takeaway
The first type of objection in jadal focuses on whether the proponent himself accepts the evidence he cites; if not, the inference is invalid because a proponent must genuinely believe his own premises.

Two Types of Objections in Jadal: Method and Context
Author lists two types of objections regarding evidence not being relied upon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of objections (i'tirad) in jadal—objecting to the method of inference and objecting to the contextual applicability of a text—and apply them to examples from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Object either because the opponent rejects his own method or because he abandons the explicit text for its implication.

In dialectical debate (jadal), not all objections are about the truth of the premise. Sometimes the objection targets the opponent's inconsistency in his own principles. This passage presents two such objections: one where the opponent uses a method he does not accept, and another where he abandons the explicit meaning (mantuq) of a hadith while clinging to its implied meaning (mafhum). By mastering these, you will sharpen your ability to detect weaknesses in legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of objections
Read the first part of the passage (type 2) and the second part (type 3). Notice that the first objection is about the method of inference (dalil al-khitab/mafhum al-mukhalafa) not being accepted by the opponent, while the second objection is about the opponent not accepting the hadith's ruling in the specific context.
Type 2 is called i'tirad 'ala al-tariq; type 3 is i'tirad 'ala al-mawdi'.
2
Analyze the first example: method disagreement
The Hanafi uses a hadith about zakat on grazing sheep to infer that fed sheep have no zakat. The Maliki objects that the Hanafi does not accept the method of implication of address (dalil al-khitab). Recognize that the objection is not about the hadith's authenticity but about the Hanafi's own methodological stance.
The Hanafi may respond by claiming he personally accepts that method, which would counter the objection.
3
Analyze the second example: contextual inconsistency
The Hanafi uses a hadith that says a free man is killed for killing his slave to argue that a free man can be killed for killing another's slave. The Maliki objects that the Hanafi does not accept the explicit ruling of the hadith (since they agree a free man is not killed for his own slave). Understand that the objection is that the opponent picks and chooses: he abandons the explicit meaning but holds onto the implied meaning.
This objection is stronger because it shows the opponent's inconsistency in accepting the same text partially.
4
Examine al-Baji's refutation of the Hanafi's answer
The Hanafi tries to answer by saying that since the hadith obligates killing for the master, it indicates an even stronger obligation for another's slave (qiyas awla). Al-Baji points out that if the explicit ruling (mantuq) is abandoned by consensus, its implication (mafhum) cannot stand alone because the implication is a branch of the explicit text. Understand the logical principle: the branch cannot survive if the root is cut.
This refutation demonstrates how jadal relies on logical relationships between explicit and implied meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - أن يستدل فيه بطريق لا يقول به :   وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنفي في أنه لا زكاة في الغنم المعلوفة بما روي عن النبي أنه قال : ( في سائمة الغنم الزكاة )، وهذا يدل على انتفاء الزكاة عن المعلوفة .   أو يستدل في إبطال خيار المجلس، بما روي عن النبي (أنه نهى عن بيع الطعام حتى يقبض) ، فدل على أنه إذا قبض في المجلس جاز بيعه . فيعترض عليه المالكي أو الحنبلي، بأن يقول : لا يصح احتجاجك بهذا الخبر، لأن وجه الدليل منه لا تقول به، وذلك أن هذا استدلال بدليل الخطاب أو مفهوم المخالفة، وأنت لا تقول به، وقد يجيب الحنفي على هذا الاعتراض بأن هذه طريقة لبعض أصحابنا وأنا ممن يقول به . . أو يقول، كما في الحديث الثاني : إن هذا اللفظ للغاية، وأنا أقول به فيما علق الحكم فيه على الغاية  3 - أن لا يقول به في الموضع الذي ورد فيه : وذلك كاستدلال الحنفي على أن الحر يقتل بالعبد، بقول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام من قتل عبده ،قتلناه ومن جدع عبده جدعناه) . فيعترض عليه المالكي أو الحنبلي بأن يقول له : القتل الذي يتناوله الخبر لا تقول به، لأنه لا خلاف بيننا وبينك أنه لا يُقتل الحر بعبده، وما يقتضيه منطوق الخبر متروك بإجماع، وقد تكلف بعض الأحناف الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض فقال : لما أوجب القتل على الحر بقتل عبده، دل على أنه يقتل بعبد غيره أولى ثم دل الدليل على أنه لا يقتل بعبده وبقى قتله بعبد غيره على ما اقتضاه وقد بين الباجي تهافت هذا الجواب فقال : هذا غلط ، لأن وجوب قتله بقتل عبد غيره مفهوم الخطاب، ووجوب قتله بعبد نفسه نفس الخطاب، فإذا سقط الخطاب، سقط المفهوم منه، ولأنه فرع له ولا يجوز أن يسقط الأصل ويبقى الفرع
2 - That he argues using a method that he does not hold to: such as the Hanafi arguing that there is no zakat on fed sheep using what was narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'On grazing sheep is zakat,' and this indicates the absence of zakat on fed sheep. Or he argues to invalidate the option of the session using what was narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that 'he forbade the sale of food until it is taken possession of,' so it indicates that if one takes possession in the session, its sale is permissible. Then the Maliki or Hanbali objects by saying: Your argument with this report is not valid, because you do not hold to the aspect of the evidence from it, and that is because this is an argument by implication of address or concept of opposition, and you do not hold to it. The Hanafi may answer this objection by saying: This is a method of some of our companions and I am among those who hold to it. Or he may say, as in the second hadith: This wording is for the limit, and I hold to it regarding what the ruling is attached to the limit. 3 - That he does not hold to it in the specific context where it is found: such as the Hanafi arguing that the free man is killed for the slave using the saying of the Prophet ﷺ: 'Whoever kills his slave, we kill him; whoever mutilates his slave, we mutilate him.' Then the Maliki or Hanbali objects by saying to him: The killing that the report covers, you do not hold to it, because there is no dispute between us that the free man is not killed for his own slave, and what the explicit meaning of the report requires is abandoned by consensus. Some Hanafis attempted to answer this objection and said: Since he made killing obligatory on the free man for killing his slave, it indicates that he is killed for another's slave by priority; then evidence indicated that he is not killed for his own slave, and his killing for another's slave remains according to what it required. Al-Baji explained the incoherence of this answer and said: This is a mistake, because the obligation of killing him for killing another's slave is the implied meaning of the address, and the obligation of killing him for his own slave is the explicit address itself; so if the address falls, its implication falls, and because it is a branch of it, and it is not permissible that the root falls and the branch remains.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition from a work on jadal (disputation) in usul al-fiqh. It presents two specific types of objections (i'tirad) that one can raise against an opponent's argument when the opponent is using a hadith or report as evidence. The first type (2) is where the opponent argues using a method of inference (such as mafhum al-mukhalafa or dalil al-khitab) that he himself does not accept in principle. The author gives two examples: the Hanafi arguing that no zakat is owed on fed sheep by using the hadith that mentions zakat on 'sa'ima' (grazing) sheep, implying that fed sheep are exempt. The Maliki or Hanbali objects that the Hanafi does not accept the method of dalil al-khitab, so his argument is self-defeating. The Hanafi may respond by claiming that he personally accepts that method, or that in the second example (about the sale of food) the wording indicates a limit (ghaya) which he accepts. The second type (3) is where the opponent does not accept the hadith in the specific context it is used. Example: the Hanafi uses a hadith that says a free man is killed if he kills his slave, to argue that a free man can be killed for killing another's slave. The Maliki objects that the Hanafi does not accept the ruling of the hadith itself (since they agree a free man is not killed for his own slave). The Hanafi's attempted answer is refuted by al-Baji, who explains that if the explicit text (mantuq) is abandoned, its implied meaning (mafhum) also falls, because the implication is a branch of the direct text. This shows the logical dependency between the explicit and implied meanings.

Key Terms
استدلال
اعتراض
دليل الخطاب
مفهوم المخالفة
منطوق
مفهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadith is weak in transmission.
B) Because the implication (mafhum) cannot stand if the explicit text (mantuq) is abandoned.
C) Because the Hanafi failed to cite a second hadith.
D) Because the Maliki is always right.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an objection can target either the opponent's methodological inconsistency or his selective use of a text, and the latter is lethal if the explicit meaning is abandoned.

Objecting by Claim of Generality (Da'wa al-Ijmal)
Author introduces a new type of objection: agreeing on the hadith's implication but disputing its purport, specifically claiming the text is general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objector uses the claim of generality (da'wa al-ijmal) against a text in a dialectical exchange and describe its logical structure.

Core Idea
Claiming a text is mujmal concedes its implication but blocks its specific application.

In jadal (dialectical debate) on usul al-fiqh, an objector can concede that a hadith's apparent meaning is one thing, but argue that it does not apply to the case at hand because the text is unclear (mujmal). This lesson unpacks the first subtype of such objections: the claim of generality (da'wa al-ijmal) against a precise text (nass).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's concession
The objector agrees on the implication (موجب) of the hadith, i.e., its apparent meaning. For example, the Maliki uses a hadith to prohibit a transaction; the objector does not deny the hadith's general meaning.
2
Locate the disputed purport
The objection disputes the purport (مقتضى) – the application of the hadith to the specific case. The objector claims the text is not clear enough to support the ruling in this instance.
3
Determine the nature of the objection
The objector claims the text is mujmal (ambiguous), meaning it requires further explanation and cannot be used as clear evidence. This is da'wa al-ijmal.
The passage lists four text types: nass (precise), zahir (apparent), 'amm (general), mujmal (ambiguous). The objector claims the text falls into the mujmal category, even if the proponent considers it nass.
4
Contrast with other text types
The objection can be directed at any text type, but here it targets a nass. Recognize that claiming ijmal against a nass is a specific strategy that forces the proponent to justify the text's clarity.
5
Examine the incomplete example
The passage begins an example: the Maliki uses a hadith from Sa'd to prohibit selling fresh dates for dry dates. The objector would claim the hadith is mujmal, so the inference is blocked. The full example would show the response.
The example is cut off; students should note that the structure is still clear.

The second demand: Objecting by saying that the implication of the hadith is agreed upon while disputing its purport. The objection here is directed at the text of the hadith itself, whether it is precise (nass), apparent (zahir), general ('amm), or ambiguous (mujmal). A - Objecting to inference from a precise text (nass): It is of two types: claim of generality (ijmal) and claim of probability (ihtimal). A-1 - Objecting to inference from a precise text by claim of generality: An example of that: that the Maliki argues for the prohibition of selling fresh dates for dry dates using what Sa'd ibn ... narrated.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section of jadal: objections based on conceding the apparent implication of the hadith but disputing its actual purport (muqtada). The objection targets the text itself, not the method. The author categorizes the text types: nass (precise), zahir (apparent), 'amm (general), mujmal (ambiguous). Then he begins the first subtype: objecting to inference from a nass by claiming the text is mujmal (general/unclear). The example given involves the Maliki using a hadith narrated by Sa'd ibn ... (the hadith is cut off) to argue that selling fresh dates for dry dates is prohibited. The complete example would show how the opponent claims the text is not clear enough to support the inference, perhaps because it is general or ambiguous. The lesson is cut off, but the structure is clear: the objector agrees that the hadith implies something, but disputes that it applies to the specific case because the text is not sufficiently precise.

Key Terms
نص (nass): A text with a clear, definitive meaning that allows no other interpretation. [Note: In usul, nass often refers to a clear injunction; some scholars restrict it to Quranic verses.]
ظاهر (zahir): An apparent meaning that is probable but admits of interpretation. [Note: Stable in usul; contrasted with nass in clarity.]
عام ('amm): A general term that encompasses all individuals of its category (e.g., 'men'). [Note: Consistent; debates about whether 'amm can be specified.]
مجملا (mujmal): An ambiguous or condensed text that requires explanation (e.g., 'establish prayer'). [Note: In this passage, mujmal is one of the categories the objection targets.]
دعوى الإجمال (da'wa al-ijmal): Claiming that a text is mujmal (ambiguous) to object to its use as evidence. [Note: Specific to jadal terminology.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim when raising an objection 'by claim of generality' (دعوى الإجمال) against a text?
A) The text is weak in transmission.
B) The text's meaning is ambiguous and not clear enough for inference.
C) The text contradicts another stronger text.
D) The text's implication is not agreed upon.

Key Takeaway
An objection by claim of generality (da'wa al-ijmal) concedes the text's apparent implication but disputes its applicability, alleging that the text is ambiguous and thus insufficient for inference.

1) In a debate about whether the hadith "لا ضرر ولا ضرار" applies to new financial products, the objector claims the text is mujmal. Two Dialectical Objections to Textual Proof: Ijmal and Ihtimal
Author enumerates and exemplifies two dialectical objections to textual proofs in usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the objection of ambiguity (ijmal) and the objection of alternative meaning (ihtimal) in a dialectical exchange, and identify the appropriate rebuttal.

Core Idea
Ambiguity attacks clarity; alternative meaning attacks the inference.

In jadal, when a textual proof is presented, the opponent may object in two ways: either the text itself is ambiguous, or it could mean something else. These are not the same. Understanding the difference helps you respond correctly. This passage from a usul al-fiqh dialectics work shows exactly how.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of objection
Find in the Arabic where the author distinguishes between دعوى الإجمال and دعوى الاحتمال. Note the context: the objection targets the text itself, not its chain or application.
The author explicitly states the two types after introducing the section.
2
Analyze the ijmal example
Read the Maliki-Hanafi exchange. The Hanafi claims the hadith 'Then no' (فلا إذا) is ambiguous because the phrase is incomplete and could mean either permission or prohibition. Identify the linguistic basis for the objection.
The Hanafi does not dispute authenticity; he disputes clarity.
3
Analyze the rebuttal to ijmal
The Maliki responds by considering the question that prompted the hadith. Combine the question and answer to determine the intended meaning. Why does this resolve ambiguity?
The rebuttal uses the principle that answers are understood relative to questions.
4
Analyze the ihtimal example
The second objection is against the hadith 'The whole earth is a mosque except the bathhouse and the cemetery.' The objection claims the text could carry a meaning different from the inferred prohibition. Note the difference: here the text is explicit (نص), but the inference is challenged.
The author notes this hadith is explicit, so the objection is weaker.
5
Compare and contrast the two objections
List the features of ijmal vs. ihtimal. Ijmal: the wording itself is unclear. Ihtimal: the wording is clear but could be interpreted differently. How does the rebuttal strategy differ?
For ijmal, you clarify the context. For ihtimal, you show the inference is the only plausible reading.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الاعتراض بالقول بموجب الحديث مع المنازعة في مقتضاه والاعتراض هنا يتوجه على متن الحديث سواء كان نصا، أو ظاهرا، أو عاما، أو مجملا . أ - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالنص : وهو على نوعين : دعوى الإجمال، ودعوى الاحتمال : أ - 1 - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالنص بدعوى الإجمال : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي في المنع من بيع الرطب بالتمر بما رواه سعد بن أبي وقاص أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن ذلك ، فقال : (أينقص الرطب إذا يبس؟) فقيل : نعم، فنهى عن ذلك، وفي رواية قال: (فلا إذا). فيعترض عليه الحنفي قائلا : هذا مجمل وليس بنص، لأن قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم (فلا إذا) غير مستقل بنفسه بل يفتقر إلى تمام فيحتمل أن يكون النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قد قصد : فلا يجوز إذا، ويحتمل أن يكون قد أراد : فلا بأس إذا ، فلا يجوز دعوى النص في مثل هذا، لأنه مجمل ويفتقر إلى البيان. وجواب المستدل عن هذا الاعتراض أن أن الحديث غير مجمل، لأن كلام النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام جاء جوابًا عن سؤال وهو أنه قيل : أيجوز ذلك أم لا ؟ فإذا قال : «فلا إذا»، وجب أن يكون هذا الجواب راجعا إلى السؤال، ومتى جمعنا بين السؤال والجواب كان الحديث نصا في المسألة . أ - ۲ - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالنص بدعوى احتماله معنى غير ما استدل فيه المستدل : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على المنع من الصلاة في المقبرة والحمام بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (الأرض كلها مسجد إلا الحمام والمقبرة) ، وهذا نص .
Second Section: The objection by saying the opposite of the hadith with dispute in its import. The objection here is directed at the text of the hadith, whether it is explicit, apparent, general, or ambiguous. A - The objection to inference by an explicit text (nass): It is of two types: claim of ambiguity (ijmal) and claim of possibility (ihtimal). A-1 - Objection to inference by an explicit text by claiming ambiguity: Its example: That the Maliki infers to forbid selling fresh dates for dried dates by what Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about that, and he said: 'Does the fresh date decrease when it dries?' It was said: Yes, so he forbade that. In another narration he said: 'Then no.' The Hanafi objects saying: This is ambiguous and not explicit, because his saying (peace be upon him) 'Then no' is not independent by itself but needs completion, so it is possible that the Prophet (peace be upon him) intended: Then it is not permissible, and possible that he intended: Then there is no harm. So it is not permissible to claim explicitness in such, because it is ambiguous and in need of clarification. The answer of the inferrer to this objection: That the hadith is not ambiguous, because the speech of the Prophet (peace be upon him) came as an answer to a question, namely it was said: Is that permissible or not? So when he said 'Then no', it is necessary that this answer be referring back to the question. And whenever we combine the question and the answer, the hadith becomes explicit in the matter. A-2 - Objection to inference by an explicit text by claiming it carries a meaning other than that for which the inferrer inferred: Its example: That the Maliki infers the prohibition of prayer in the cemetery and the bathhouse by what was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The whole earth is a mosque except the bathhouse and the cemetery.' And this is explicit.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition from a work on jadal (dialectics) applied to usul al-fiqh. It falls under the second section (المطلب الثاني) which deals with the objection 'by saying the opposite of the hadith with dispute in its import.' Here, the author specifies that such an objection targets the text itself (متن الحديث) regardless of whether it is explicit (نص), apparent (ظاهر), general (عام), or ambiguous (مجمَل). The focus then narrows to objection against inference by an explicit text (الاستدلال بالنص). Two types are distinguished: (1) claiming ambiguity (دعوى الإجمال) and (2) claiming an alternative meaning (دعوى الاحتمال). The author provides a detailed example for each. For the first type, the Maliki argues that selling fresh dates for dried dates is forbidden based on a hadith where the Prophet said 'Then no' (فلا إذا) in response to a question. The Hanafi objects that this phrase is not independent and could mean either 'Then it is not permissible' or 'Then there is no harm,' making the hadith ambiguous. The Maliki's rebuttal is that the Prophet's words are an answer to a specific question ('Is that permissible or not?'), so when combined with the question the meaning becomes clear and explicit. For the second type, the Maliki argues that prayer in a cemetery or bathhouse is forbidden based on the hadith 'The whole earth is a mosque except the bathhouse and the cemetery.' The objection here is not ambiguity but that the text might carry a different meaning than the Maliki inferred; however, the author notes that this hadith is explicit (نص), implying that the objection would be invalid. The passage demonstrates how dialectical objections in fiqh rely on precise linguistic and contextual analysis.

Key Terms
إجمال
احتمال
نص

B) The phrase 'Then no' is not independent and could mean either permission or prohibition.
C) The hadith contradicts a stronger textual evidence.
D) The context of the hadith shows it was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, claiming ambiguity (ijmal) targets the clarity of the text itself, while claiming alternative meaning (ihtimal) targets the inference drawn from a clear text; each requires a different rebuttal strategy.
