Objecting by Reinterpreting According to Sharʿī Custom
First method of objecting to apparent meaning by carrying to Sharʿī custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objector is reinterpreting a term according to Sharʿī custom and evaluate whether the move is justified.

Core Idea
Apparent meaning can be challenged by showing that the term's Sharʿī usage differs from its linguistic use.

In Islamic legal debate, apparent meaning is not absolute. An objector can argue that the intended meaning is the Sharʿī custom, not the linguistic one. This lesson examines this first method with a real fiqh example from slaughter (dhabīḥa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's apparent meaning
Find the term the arguer relies on and its apparent linguistic meaning. In the example, the Mālikī uses 'al-nahr' (slaughter) in the ḥadīth without mentioning basmala.
2
Recognize the objector's claim
The objector claims that the term should be understood according to Sharʿī custom, not the linguistic sense. The Ḥanafī says 'al-nahr' here means legal slaughter, which includes basmala.
3
Assess evidence for the custom
Does the objector provide proof for the existence of such a custom? The Ḥanafī must show that Sharīʿa custom requires basmala in slaughter.
4
Determine the arguer's response
The Mālikī counters by showing no such custom exists. The response is effective if it demonstrates absence of custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالظاهر : والظاهر ثلاثة أنواع : ظاهر بالوضع، وظاهر بالعرف، وظاهر بالدلالة، وكل واحد منها يتوجه الاعتراض على الاستدلال به. ب - 1 - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالظاهر بالوضع : والذي يخصه من وجوه المنازعة أمران : أحدهما : أن يحمله المعترض على عرف الشرع . والثاني : أن يحمله على غير المعنى الذي حمله المستدل عليه في اللغة . فأما الحمل على عرف الشرع، فمثاله : أن يستدل بعض المالكيين على أن التسمية ليست بشرط في صحة الذكاة بما رواه البراء بن عازب عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (أول ما نبدأ به في يومنا هذا أن نصلي، ثم نرجع فنتحر فمن فعل ذلك فقد أصاب)، ولم يذكر التسمية. فيقول الحنفي معترضا : إنما أراد بالنحر هنا النحر الشرعي وهو يشمل التسمية والذكاة معا، لكن متى تعمد الذابح أو الناحر ترك التسمية فليست بذكاة شرعية، فلا يصح الاحتجاج بهذا الحديث في مثل هذا الموضع . والطريق للمالكي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يبين ألا عرف في الشريعة يقتضي التسمية في الذبح والنحر . 2 - وأما حمل المعترض اللفظ على غير ما حمله عليه المستدل في اللغة، فهو ثلاثة أنواع : الأول : أن يكون اللفظ موضوعا لمعنيين هو في أحدهما أظهر : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن فضيلة إتيان الجمعة لا تكون في أول النهار، بما رواه أبو هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي الله أنه قال : (إذا كان يوم الجمعة كان على كل باب من أبواب المسجد ملائكة يكتبون الأول فالأول، فإذا جلس الإمام طووا الصحف وجاؤوا يستمعون الذكر، ومثل المهجر كالذي يهدي البدنة، ثم كالذي يهدي بقرة، ثم كالذي يهدي الكبش ثم كالذي يهدي الدجاجة ثم كالذي يهدي البيضة) ، ووجه الاستدلال منه أنه جعل أعلى الفضيلة هو إهداء البدنة للمهجر، وذلك لا ينطبق على من أتى الجمعة في أول النهار، فإنما يقال له : مبكر، وغاد، ولا يقال إلا لمن أتاها في الهاجرة، فيقول الشافعي معترضا : يحتمل أن يكون المراد بالمهجر : الذي يهجر منزله ويأتي الجمعة. وجواب المالكي عن ذلك أن يبين أن لفظة (المهجر) أظهر في المعنى الذي استدل عليه فمن يترك منزله يقال له : هجر منزله ولا يقال عنه : هجر، فلا يعدل عن المعنى الظاهر إلا بدليل . الثاني : أن يكون اللفظ موضوعا لمعنيين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي في طلاق المكره بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (لا طلاق ولا عتاق في
B - Objection to argument by apparent meaning: And apparent meaning is three types: apparent by convention, apparent by custom, and apparent by indication. And each one of them may be objected to. B-1 - Objection to argument by apparent meaning by convention: And what specifically pertains to it from aspects of dispute are two matters: The first: that the objector carries it to the custom of the Law. The second: that he carries it to a meaning other than the one the arguer carried it to in the language. As for carrying it to the custom of the Law, its example is that some Malikis argue that the basmala is not a condition for the validity of slaughter based on what al-Bara' ibn 'Azib narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The first thing we begin with on this day of ours is that we pray, then we return and slaughter. Whoever does that has hit the mark.' And he did not mention the basmala. So the Hanafi says as an objection: He only meant by 'slaughter' here the legal slaughter, which includes the basmala and the slaughter together. But whenever the slaughterer or sacrificer intentionally omits the basmala, it is not a legal slaughter. So it is not valid to use this hadith as evidence in such a case. And the way for the Maliki to answer this objection is to show that there is no custom in the Shari'a that requires the basmala in slaughter and sacrifice. 2 - As for the objector carrying the wording to something other than what the arguer carried it to in the language, it is of three types: The first: that the word is placed for two meanings, in one of which it is more apparent. An example: that the Maliki argues that the virtue of coming to Friday prayer is not at the beginning of the day, based on what Abu Huraira (may Allah be pleased with him) narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'On Friday, at every door of the mosque there are angels writing down the first and the next. When the imam sits, they fold up the scrolls and come to listen to the remembrance. And the like of the muhajjir (one who comes at midday) is like one who sacrifices a camel, then like one who sacrifices a cow, then like one who sacrifices a ram, then like one who sacrifices a chicken, then like one who sacrifices an egg.' And the point of argument from it is that he made the highest virtue be the offering of a camel for the muhajjir, and that does not apply to one who comes to Friday prayer at the beginning of the day, for such a person is called mubakkir (early) and ghadin (morning), and is not called muhajjir except for one who comes at midday (al-hajira). So the Shafi'i says as an objection: It is possible that the meaning of muhajjir is the one who leaves his home and comes to Friday prayer. And the Maliki's answer to that is to show that the word (al-muhajjir) is more apparent in the meaning he argued for, because one who leaves his home is said: 'he left (hajara) his home' and it is not said about him: 'he came at midday (hajjara)', so one does not turn away from the apparent meaning except with evidence. The second: that the word is placed for two meanings with no advantage for one over the other. Its example: that the Maliki argues concerning the divorce of the coerced person by the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'There is no divorce and no manumission in
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is from a manual of dialectical argumentation (jadal). It explains how an objector can challenge an argument that relies on the apparent meaning (al-ẓāhir) of a text. The author first classifies apparent meaning into three types: apparent by linguistic convention (al-waḍʿ), apparent by custom (al-ʿurf), and apparent by indication (al-dalāla). Then he focuses on the first type, objection to argument from apparent meaning by convention. Under this, there are two specific ways to object. The first way is to reinterpret the term according to the custom of the Sharīʿa rather than its linguistic sense. The example given is from the dispute between Mālikīs and Ḥanafīs about the validity of slaughter without the basmala. The Mālikī argues from a ḥadīth where the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) described the Eid procedure without mentioning the basmala. The Ḥanafī objects by saying that the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) meant 'legal slaughter' (al-nahr al-sharʿī), which includes the basmala. The Mālikī's response is to argue that there is no Sharʿī custom requiring the basmala in slaughter. The second way is to carry the term to a different linguistic meaning than the one the arguer intended. The author then breaks this down into three subtypes (though the text only covers the first two completely). The first subtype is when a word has two meanings, one of which is more apparent (aẓhar). The example: the Mālikī argues that the virtue of coming to Friday prayer is highest at midday (al-hājira) based on a ḥadīth that mentions the muhajjir. The Shāfiʿī objects by saying that muhajjir could mean 'one who leaves his home' (from hajara). The Mālikī counters that muhajjir is more apparent in the sense of coming at midday, because the verb for leaving home is hajara, not hajjara. The second subtype is when a word has two meanings equally apparent. The example, which is cut off, concerns the Mālikī arguing about divorce under coercion using the ḥadīth 'lā ṭalāqa wa lā ʿitāqa fī...' The passage teaches that in dialectical debate, one must be precise about the apparentness (ẓuhūr) of terms and that an objector can challenge an argument by offering a different interpretation if it is supported by Sharʿī custom or linguistic evidence.

Key Terms

الحمل على عرف الشرع

Key Takeaway

Objecting by Offering an Alternative Linguistic Meaning
Second method of objecting to apparent meaning by carrying to a different linguistic meaning, focusing on first two subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between objections based on a word having two meanings with one being more apparent vs. equally apparent, and formulate appropriate responses.

Core Idea
An objector can challenge an apparent meaning by offering a different linguistic interpretation, but must argue why that interpretation is valid.

The second method of objection involves reinterpreting the term in a different linguistic sense. The text distinguishes three subtypes, though only the first two are fully covered. This lesson focuses on the first subtype where one meaning is more apparent, and the second where meanings are equally apparent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term in dispute
Find the term whose apparent meaning is being challenged. In the Friday prayer example, it is 'al-muhajjir'.
2
Determine the term's multiple meanings
Check if the term has two or more linguistic meanings. 'Muhajjir' can mean 'one who comes at midday' (from hajjara) or 'one who leaves his home' (from hajara).
3
Classify the relation between meanings
Is one meaning more apparent (aẓhar) than the other, or are they equally apparent? In the example, the author argues the midday meaning is more apparent.
4
Evaluate the objector's argument and response
The objector proposes the less apparent meaning; the arguer defends the more apparent meaning. The arguer's response should show that turning away from the apparent meaning requires evidence.

First Type of Dialectical Objection: Word with More Apparent Meaning
Defines the first type of objection where the word has two meanings, one more apparent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the first type of dialectical objection where a word has two meanings and one is more apparent, using the example of 'muhajjir' in the Friday prayer hadith.

Core Idea
When a word has two meanings, the apparent meaning holds until evidence overrides it.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one common objection is to reinterpret a key word differently from the proponent. But not all reinterpretations are equal: if a word has two meanings and one is clearly more apparent, the apparent meaning must be followed unless the objector brings strong evidence. This lesson examines the first type of such objection through a classic juristic example between the Maliki and Shafi'i schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed word
Locate the word in the argument that the objector interprets differently. In this case, the word is 'al-muhajjir' in the hadith about Friday prayer.
The word appears in the Prophet's statement: 'Muhajjir is like one who sacrifices a camel...'
2
Determine the two possible meanings
The Maliki argues that 'muhajjir' means one who comes at midday (hajira). The Shafi'i proposes an alternative: one who leaves his home (hajara). List both meanings.
The root h-j-r has senses of 'leaving' and 'migration', but also 'coming at midday'.
3
Assess which meaning is more apparent (azhar)
Check linguistic usage: the pattern 'mufa''il' (muhajjir) typically indicates the one who performs the action. For 'leaving home', the verb 'hajara' is used, but the active participle is 'hajir' not 'muhajjir'. Thus 'muhajjir' is more apparent in the sense of coming at midday.
The text states: 'one who leaves his home is said to 'hajara his home', but one who does that is not called 'hajr'; so the apparent meaning is preferred.'
4
Understand the objector's challenge
The Shafi'i objects by suggesting the alternative meaning is possible, even if less apparent. The objector does not need to prove his meaning is more apparent; he only needs to show the word could bear that meaning.
In jadal, the burden of proof shifts to the proponent to defend the apparent meaning.
5
Evaluate the response to the objection
The Maliki responds by demonstrating that the apparent meaning is not abandoned except with evidence (dalil). Since the objector offered no evidence, the apparent meaning stands. This is the rule: 'the apparent meaning is not abandoned except with evidence.'
This establishes a principle: the apparent meaning (zahir) has priority in debate unless a clear proof overrides it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حمل المعترض اللفظ على غير ما حمله عليه المستدل في اللغة، فهو ثلاثة أنواع : الأول : أن يكون اللفظ موضوعا لمعنيين هو في أحدهما أظهر : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن فضيلة إتيان الجمعة لا تكون في أول النهار، بما رواه أبو هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي الله أنه قال : (إذا كان يوم الجمعة كان على كل باب من أبواب المسجد ملائكة يكتبون الأول فالأول، فإذا جلس الإمام طووا الصحف وجاؤوا يستمعون الذكر، ومثل المهجر كالذي يهدي البدنة، ثم كالذي يهدي بقرة، ثم كالذي يهدي الكبش ثم كالذي يهدي الدجاجة ثم كالذي يهدي البيضة) (2) ، ووجه الاستدلال منه أنه جعل أعلى الفضيلة هو إهداء البدنة للمهجر، وذلك لا ينطبق على من أتى الجمعة في أول النهار، فإنما يقال له : مبكر، وغاد، ولا يقال إلا لمن أتاها في الهاجرة، فيقول الشافعي معترضا : يحتمل أن يكون المراد بالمهجر : الذي يهجر منزله ويأتي الجمعة. وجواب المالكي عن ذلك أن يبين أن لفظة (المهجر) أظهر في المعنى الذي استدل عليه فمن يترك منزله يقال له : هجر منزله ولا يقال عنه : هجر، فلا يعدل عن المعنى الظاهر إلا بدليل
As for the objector's interpretation of the word differently from what the proponent interpreted it in the language, it is of three types: The first: that the word is assigned to two meanings and it is more apparent in one of them. An example is that the Maliki argues that the virtue of attending Friday prayer does not occur at the beginning of the day, based on what Abu Hurairah narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'On Friday, at every door of the mosque there are angels who write down the first and then the next; when the imam sits, they fold up the scrolls and come to listen to the remembrance. One who comes early (muhajjir) is like one who sacrifices a camel, then a cow, then a ram, then a chicken, then an egg.' The evidence from this is that he made the highest virtue the sacrifice of a camel for the muhajjir, and that does not apply to one who comes to Friday prayer at the beginning of the day, for such a one is called mubakkir or ghadi, and not muhajjir, which is only said of one who comes at midday (hajira). So the Shafi'i objects: It is possible that what is meant by al-muhajjir is one who leaves his home and comes to Friday prayer. The Maliki's answer to that is to show that the word (al-muhajjir) is more apparent in the meaning he argued for, because one who leaves his home is said to 'hajara his home', but one who does that is not called 'hajr'; so the apparent meaning is not abandoned except with evidence.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of dialectical objection where the objector interprets the word differently from the proponent, specifically when the word has two possible meanings but one is clearly more apparent. The author provides a juristic example: the Maliki uses the hadith about the virtue of coming early to Friday prayer to argue that the highest virtue is not at the beginning of the day but at midday, because the word 'muhajjir' is used for one who comes at the time of hajira (midday). The Shafi'i objects by suggesting an alternative meaning: 'muhajjir' could be one who leaves his home (hajara). The Maliki's response is that the word 'muhajjir' is more apparent in the sense of coming at midday because the verbal form 'hajara' (with the meaning of leaving) is not used in the pattern 'muhajjir'. Therefore, the established apparent meaning is preferred, and one does not depart from it without a clear evidence (dalil). This demonstrates a key principle in jadal: the apparent meaning (zahir) is given priority unless there is a strong reason to interpret otherwise.

Key Terms
المهجر (al-muhajjir): The one who comes to Friday prayer at midday (hajira), as opposed to one who leaves his home; the term is argued to have a more apparent meaning in the hadith. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, 'muhajjir' typically refers to one who goes to Friday prayer early at midday; this usage is consistent.]
المعنى الظاهر (al-ma'na al-zahir): The more obvious meaning when a word has multiple senses; it is preferred unless there is evidence to leave it. [Note: Stable across usul al-fiqh and jadal.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both meanings are taken equally.
B) The less apparent meaning is preferred.
C) The apparent meaning is preferred unless there is evidence to the contrary.
D) The word is considered ambiguous and discarded.

Key Takeaway
The apparent meaning (zahir) of a word takes precedence in dialectical debate unless there is evidence to the contrary.

Resolving Ambiguity: Two Equal Meanings
Defines the second type where the word has two meanings with no preference, and gives the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the jadal rule for words with two equally likely meanings by identifying when both meanings can be accepted simultaneously.

Core Idea
When a word equally fits two meanings, take both — unless they contradict.

In dialectical argument (jadal), opponents often dispute the meaning of a key term. What happens when a word has two meanings and neither is more likely? The classical usuli rule, shown here with the Maliki-Hanafi debate on ighlaq, is that both meanings can be adopted — as long as they are not contradictory. This lesson walks you through that rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
Find the word that both parties agree is the focal point. In the example, it is 'ighlaq' in the hadith. The Maliki interprets it as coercion (ikrah); the Hanafi as madness (junun).
The term must be one that can bear both meanings lexically.
2
Check if one meaning is preferred
The text says there is 'no advantage of one over the other'. If there were, the preferred meaning would be taken. Here both are equally plausible.
This is the condition for this type: equal probability (tasawi).
3
Determine if the meanings conflict
The rule allows both meanings only if they are not contradictory (mutanāfin). In the example, coercion and madness are not mutually exclusive — a person can be both coerced and mad. So they can coexist.
Contradiction would be like 'day' and 'night' for the same word.
4
Apply the rule: use both meanings
If the word applies to both and they are non-contradictory, the proponent can argue that the ruling applies under both interpretations. The Maliki can say the hadith covers coerced divorce because ighlaq includes ikrah, and the Hanafi's objection that it means junun does not invalidate the Maliki's claim — rather, the hadith encompasses both.
This strengthens the argument: the proof covers more cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن يكون اللفظ موضوعا لمعنيين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي في طلاق المكره بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (لا طلاق ولا عتاق في إغلاق) (1) ، والإغلاق هو الإكراه، فيقول الحنفي معترضا: الإغلاق هو الجنون . وجواب المستدل عن هذا أن يقول : إذا وقع الاسم على الأمرين جميعا، حمل عليهما دون أن يكون هناك تناف بينهما، لأن حمل اللفظ الواحد على معنيين مختلفين غير متضادين جائز (2)
The second: that the word is assigned to two meanings with no advantage of one over the other. An example: the Maliki argues regarding the divorce of the coerced person using the Prophet's ﷺ statement: 'There is no divorce and no manumission in ighlaq.' Ighlaq is coercion. So the Hanafi objects: Ighlaq is madness. The proponent's answer to this is to say: If the name applies to both matters, it is taken as applying to both, as long as there is no contradiction between them, because it is permissible to use a single word for two different non-conflicting meanings.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of objection: when the word has two meanings that are equally likely (no one is more apparent). The example is the hadith about divorce under compulsion: 'لا طلاق ولا عتاق في إغلاق'. The Maliki uses this to argue that coerced divorce is invalid, interpreting 'ighlaq' as coercion (ikrah). The Hanafi objects that 'ighlaq' means madness (junun). The proponent's answer is that since the term is equally applicable to both meanings and they are not contradictory (coercion and madness can both prevent valid divorce), the word can be taken to encompass both. This is permissible because a single word can carry multiple non-contradictory senses. The lesson highlights that in jadal, when there is ambiguity with equal weight, both meanings can be adopted simultaneously, provided they are not mutually exclusive.

الإغلاق
المعنيين المتساويين
لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر
جواب المستدل

A) Only the first meaning is taken.

C) Both meanings are taken if they are not contradictory.
D) The word is considered invalid and the argument fails.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a word with two equally likely meanings can be applied to both, provided they are not contradictory, turning an objection into a broader proof.

Handling Definitional Disputes in Jadal
Demonstrates a jadali objection and response regarding the term 'ighlaq'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a jadali objection based on redefining a term and evaluate the response using the principle of permissible multiple meanings.

Core Idea
A term can have multiple non-contradictory meanings without conflict.

In jadal (dialectic), a common move is to challenge the definition of a key term. Here, the Hanafi objector redefines 'ighlaq' as 'madness' instead of 'coercion'. The respondent counters with a logical principle: if the term applies to both meanings without contradiction, it can be used for both. This lesson unpacks this exchange and its implications for definitional disputes in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
Find the original definition of 'ighlaq' in the passage. It is stated as 'coercion' by the respondent (mustadill).
2
Locate the objection
The Hanafi objector redefines 'ighlaq' as 'madness'. This is a ta'yin objection: claiming the term means something else.
This type of objection targets the meaning of a key term.
3
Understand the response
The respondent argues that if the word 'ighlaq' applies to both matters without contradiction, it can be used for both. This is a principle for handling homonyms (mushtarak).
4
Evaluate the logical strength
Consider whether the two meanings (coercion and madness) are truly non-contradictory. In what context could they coexist? This step checks the validity of the response.
The passage assumes they are not contradictory; critical thinking may question this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإغلاق هو الإكراه، فيقول الحنفي معترضا: الإغلاق هو الجنون . وجواب المستدل عن هذا أن يقول : إذا وقع الاسم على الأمرين جميعا، حمل عليهما دون أن يكون هناك تناف بينهما، لأن حمل اللفظ الواحد على معنيين مختلفين غير متضادين جائز
Al-ighlaq is coercion. The Hanafi objects saying: Al-ighlaq is madness. The respondent's answer to this is to say: If the name applies to both matters, it is carried upon both without there being contradiction between them, because it is permissible to carry a single word upon two different non-contradictory meanings.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents a jadali exchange regarding the term 'ighlaq'. The original claim defines 'ighlaq' as 'coercion'. The interlocutor (Hanafi) objects by redefining 'ighlaq' as 'madness', thus undermining the original meaning. The response is a logical principle: if a word has multiple meanings that are not mutually exclusive, it can be applied to all of them. This guards against the fallacy of equivocation (mushtarak) when the word's common meaning is not contradictory. For example, in fiqh, 'wudu'' can mean washing or the act, but both are valid contexts. The commentator emphasizes that the same word can carry different meanings without conflict.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an objection that redefines a key term can be answered by showing the term has multiple non-contradictory meanings, and the dispute is resolved by acknowledging the term's homonymy.

Resolving Disputes by Distinguishing Terms: 'Araya vs 'Ariyah
Presents a jadali objection and response on the meaning of 'araya in hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a jadal dispute by identifying the objection, the response, and the key terminological distinction that resolves it.

Core Idea
The meaning of a word can decide a legal dispute, but precision is required.

In jadal, disputants often argue over the import of a key term. In this passage, the Maliki and Hanafi schools dispute the meaning of 'araya in a hadith about selling dates. The Maliki's response hinges on distinguishing 'araya from 'ariyah, showing that careful linguistic analysis can resolve legal disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputants and their positions
Find who is making the objection and who responds. The Hanafi objects that 'araya means gifts; the Maliki defends that it means a sale.
2
Locate the key term in dispute
The term 'araya is central. Note how each school interprets it differently.
3
Analyse the objection
The Hanafi objects that the hadith is not proof because 'araya means gifts (hibat), not sales.
4
Analyse the response
The Maliki distinguishes 'araya (a palm tree given to a needy person for a year) from 'ariyah (loan of benefits), and shows the hadith permits sale by estimate, not gift.
5
Assess the outcome by jadal rules
The Maliki's response is effective because it clarifies the meaning and shows inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن يتنازعا في مقتضاه فيدعي كل منهما أنه موضوع لما يدعيه : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على بيع العرايا بخرصها تمرّا من المعري إلى جذاذها، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه نهى عن بيع الرطب بالتمر، إلا أنه رخص في العرايا أن تباع بخرصها تمرًا يأكلها أهلها رطبًا فيما دون خمسة أوسق فيقول الحنفي معترضا : ليس في الحديث حجة لكم، وذلك لأن معنى العرايا الهبات والعطايا من الإعارة وهى هبة المنافع، وقد أجاز ذلك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الرجل يهب الرجل تمر نخلة ثم يرجع في هبته قبل أن يجذها الموهب له، ويهبه تمرًا غير ذلك وهذا جائز . . طريق المالكي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يبين أن العرايا ليست من الهبات في شيء، ثم يبين أن في الحديث ما يمنع من حمله على ما ادعى المعترض، فالعرايا واحدتها ،عرية وليس عارية فالعرية هي النخلة التي يُغريها صاحبها محتاجًا، والإعراء أن يجعل له ثمرتها عاما، وأما العارية فهي بذل المنافع، فالعرية غير العارية فبطل ما قاله المعترض ثم إن في الحديث أيضًا ما يمنع مما أوله به المعترض، وذلك أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أرخص في العرايا أن تباع بخرصها، وعلى ما قاله المعترض لا يكون بيعًا وإنما يكون هبة مبتدأة
The third: that they dispute over its implication, each claiming that it is used for what he asserts. For example, the Maliki uses as evidence the sale of 'araya by estimating their amount as dates from the giver to their taker, based on the narration that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade selling fresh dates for dried dates, but he permitted 'araya to be sold by estimate as dates for the family to eat fresh, up to five wasaq. The Hanafi objects saying: There is no proof in the hadith for you, because the meaning of 'araya is gifts and donations from loan, and that is a gift of benefits; the Messenger of Allah allowed that regarding a man giving a man a date palm and then taking back his gift before the recipient takes the dates, and he gives him other dates. That is permissible. The Maliki's method of answering this objection is to show that 'araya are not gifts at all, then to show that in the hadith there is something that prevents carrying it upon what the objector claimed. For 'araya, its singular is 'ariyyah and not 'ariyah. Al-'ariyyah is a palm tree whose owner gives it to a needy person, and al-i'ra' is to give him its fruit for a year. As for al-'ariyah, it is the giving of benefits. So al-'ariyyah is different from al-'ariyah, thus what the objector said is invalid. Moreover, in the hadith there is also that which prevents his interpretation: the Prophet permitted 'araya to be sold by estimate, and according to the objector it would not be a sale but rather an initial gift.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage illustrates the third type of dispute in jadal: disputing the import (muqtada) of a term. The Maliki school uses the hadith about 'araya to allow selling fresh dates for dried dates. The Hanafi objects that 'araya does not mean sale but gifts (hibat). The Maliki responds by distinguishing between 'araya (a specific palm tree made available to a needy person) and 'ariyah (usufruct gift). He then shows that the hadith itself indicates a sale, not a gift, because the Prophet permitted selling by estimate. This demonstrates the technique of rebutting an objection by clarifying the true meaning of a word and showing the inconsistency of the objector's interpretation.

العارية
الهِبة

Key Takeaway
Disputing the import of a word is a common jadal tactic, but a solid linguistic distinction can effectively rebut an objection.

Objecting to Apparent Meaning by Shari'ah Custom
Explains how to object to an argument using the apparent meaning in shari'ah custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct an objection to an argument based on apparent meaning by appealing to shari'ah custom, using the example of 'maytah'.

Core Idea
In jadal, when a term's shari'ah meaning differs from its linguistic meaning, the shari'ah custom takes precedence.

This lesson covers a specific objection technique in dialectical debate (jadal): challenging an argument that relies on the apparent meaning (zahir) of a text by appealing to the fact that the term in shari'ah custom may have a narrower meaning than its linguistic sense. The example involves the Maliki argument that meat from an animal cut while alive is haram because the hadith calls it 'dead.' The Shafi'i objects that in language 'dead' includes slaughtered animals, which are halal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and proof text
The Maliki claims that meat from an animal that had a limb cut off while alive is impermissible. Proof: the hadith 'Whatever is cut from a live animal is dead.'
2
Recognize the objector's move
The Shafi'i objects that the term 'dead' linguistically includes animals that have been properly slaughtered, which are permissible. Thus the hadith does not prove impermissibility.
This is an objection by carrying the term to its linguistic implication.
3
Analyze the respondent's counter-objection
The Maliki responds that in shari'ah usage, 'maytah' (dead) is restricted to animals that died unlawfully, not those killed by proper slaughter. He supports this with Quranic verses and a hadith that specify what 'maytah' means in shari'ah.
The respondent appeals to shari'ah custom to narrow the meaning.
4
Understand the rule
The objection to apparent meaning by shari'ah custom succeeds when the term has a technical sense that differs from its linguistic sense, and the context indicates the technical sense is intended.
This move preserves the authority of shari'ah terminology over common language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - 2 - الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالظاهر بالعرف . والظاهر بالعرف على ضربين ظاهر بعرف الشرع، وظاهر بعرف اللغة : فأما الظاهر بعرف الشرع، فيعترض عليه بأن يحمله المعترض على مقتضاه في اللغة، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن اليد إذا قطعت من الحي فإنه لا يحل أكلها بما روي عن النبي يا الله أنه قال : (ما قطع من حي فهو میت)، وهذا يدل على أنه لا يجوز أكله فيعترضه الشافعي قائلا : ليس في هذا ما يدل على أنه لا يجوز أكله، لأن المذكى ميت ويجوز أكله، والميت اسم لكل ما فقد منه الروح، سواء كان ذلك بذكاة أو بغير ذكاة، وجواب المستدل هنا أن يقول : أن الميت إذا أطلق في الشرع، فإنما ينصرف إلى ما لا يجوز أكله، والدليل على ذلك قوله تعالى : حُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمُ الْمَيْتَةُ . . . (3) وقوله عز وجل : و إِلَّا أَن يَكُونَ مَيْتَةً أَوْ دَمًا مَّسْفُوحًا . . . (4) ، وقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: أحلت لكم ميتتان: السمك والجراد) (5) ، ولو كان وصف الميت يطلق على المذكى لم يصح هذا الكلام
B-2: Objection to inference by the apparent meaning according to custom. The apparent meaning by custom is of two types: apparent by shari'ah custom and apparent by linguistic custom. As for the apparent by shari'ah custom, it is objected by the objector carrying it upon its implication in language. For example, the Maliki uses as evidence that if a limb is cut from a live animal, it is not permissible to eat it, based on the Prophet's saying: 'Whatever is cut from a live animal is dead.' The Shafi'i objects saying: This does not indicate that it is not permissible to eat it, because an animal that is slaughtered (mudhakka) is dead and permissible to eat, and 'dead' is a name for everything that lacks life, whether by slaughter or otherwise. The respondent's answer here is to say: When 'dead' is used in shari'ah, it only applies to what is not permissible to eat. The evidence is His saying: 'Forbidden to you is carrion...' and His saying: 'Unless it be dead or spilled blood...' and the Prophet said: 'Two dead things are made lawful for you: fish and locust.' If the description 'dead' applied to the slaughtered, this speech would not be valid.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage introduces a specific type of objection in jadal: objecting to an argument based on the apparent meaning of a text by appealing to shari'ah custom. The example involves the Maliki argument that cutting a limb from a live animal renders it impermissible because the hadith calls it 'dead'. The Shafi'i objects by noting that in language, 'dead' includes slaughtered animals, which are permissible. The Maliki responds that in shari'ah, the term 'dead' (maytah) has a specific meaning: it refers to animals not killed by proper slaughter, as supported by Quranic verses and hadith. This shows that technical terms in shari'ah may have a narrower meaning than their linguistic origin, and one must respect that custom when interpreting proof texts.

Key Terms
الظاهر بالعرف
الميتة
المذكى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In jadal, when a term's shari'ah custom meaning differs from its linguistic meaning, the shari'ah custom takes precedence in interpreting proof texts.

Disputing Apparent Meaning by Linguistic Custom
Author defines the second type of objection: disputing the apparent meaning via linguistic custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a munaza'a bi 'urf al-lugha objection and construct the corresponding response in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Linguistic custom can override the apparent meaning when the text's general term is customarily restricted.

In jadal, when a claimant relies on an apparent meaning (zahir), the objector can dispute it by appealing to linguistic custom (urf al-lugha). This lesson uses the classic Maliki-Hanafi debate on Zakat al-Fitr to show how this objection works and how the respondent defends the apparent meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent meaning (zahir) being claimed
Find the specific interpretation the claimant assigns to a word or phrase in the text. In the example, the Maliki claims that 'food' (ta'am) in the hadith refers specifically to wheat.
2
Formulate the objection based on linguistic custom
The objector argues that the term in question has a broader range in the customary usage of the language ('urf al-lugha), so the apparent meaning cannot be exclusive. The Hanafi says 'food' includes dates, butter, etc., not just wheat.
3
Analyze the respondent's counter
The respondent must show that linguistic custom actually restricts the term in this context. Here, the Maliki notes that the hadith already lists other foods separately, so the general 'food' necessarily refers to wheat, and that 'urf al-lugha treats wheat as the primary referent.
The respondent combines custom with textual evidence (the listing of other foods).
4
Assess the strength of the objection and response
This type of objection is valid only if the linguistic custom is genuinely established and the text's context supports the restriction. The Maliki's response is considered strong because it appeals to a known custom and internal textual clues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : المنازعة في الظاهر بعرف اللغة ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على أن الواجب في صدقة الفطر صاع من قمح، بما روي عن أبي سعيد أنه قال : «كنا نخرج - إذ فينا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم - زكاة الفطر عن كل صغير وكبير، حر أو مملوك، صاعا من طعام أو صاعا أقط ، أو صاعا من شعير، صاعا من تمر، أو صاعا زبيب» . فيقول الحنفي معترضا اسم الطعام واقع على كل ما يتطعم من تمر وسمن وغير ذلك، وليس في الحديث ما يدل على أن المراد به البر دون غيره. وجواب المستدل أن يبين أن عرف هذه اللفظة في اللغة يقول : إن ظاهر هذا اللفظ إذا أطلق يتناول البر، وإن كان معنى الطعم فيه غير موجود ؛ ثم إن الحديث قد تضمن سائر أنواع الأقوات، فلم يبق ما يحمل عليه اسم الطعام إلا البر.
The second: disputing the apparent meaning by linguistic custom. Its example: the Maliki argues that the obligatory [payment] in Zakat al-Fitr is a sa' of wheat, based on what was narrated from Abu Sa'id that he said: 'We used to give – when the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was among us – Zakat al-Fitr for every young and old, free and slave, a sa' of food or a sa' of dried yogurt, or a sa' of barley, or a sa' of dates, or a sa' of raisins.' The Hanafi objects saying: the noun 'food' applies to everything that is eaten from dates, clarified butter, and other things, and there is nothing in the hadith indicating that what is meant by it is wheat to the exclusion of others. The respondent's answer is that he clarifies that the custom of this word in the language states: the apparent meaning of this word, when used absolutely, covers wheat, even if the meaning of edibility is not present in it. Then the hadith has included all other types of provisions, so nothing remains to which the noun 'food' can be applied except wheat.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of objection (i'tirad) in jadal: 'disputing the apparent meaning by linguistic custom' (al-munaza'a fi al-zahir bi 'urf al-lugha). The author gives a concrete example from fiqh: the Maliki argues that Zakat al-Fitr must be paid in wheat (burr) based on a hadith that mentions 'food' (ta'am). The Hanafi objects that the word 'food' linguistically includes many types of foodstuffs, not just wheat. The Maliki responds by clarifying that the customary usage (urf) of the word 'food' in the Arabic language, when used absolutely, primarily denotes wheat, even though the broader meaning of edibility is present. Moreover, the hadith already lists other types of provisions separately, so the unspecified 'food' must refer to wheat. This illustrates how disputing the apparent meaning by appealing to custom can shift the understanding of a text.

Key Terms
المنازعة في الظاهر بعرف اللغة
الظاهر
عرف اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Maliki-Hanafi dispute about Zakat al-Fitr, how does the Maliki respondent counter the Hanafi's objection that 'food' (ta'am) includes many things?
A) He argues that the hadith is weak and cannot be used as evidence.
B) He claims that linguistic custom (urf) restricts 'food' primarily to wheat, and the hadith already mentions other foods separately.
C) He asserts that the word 'food' in the Quran only means wheat.
D) He changes the subject to the intention of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The munaza'a bi 'urf al-lugha objection challenges the apparent meaning by appealing to the broader linguistic usage of a term, and the respondent defends by showing that the linguistic custom actually restricts the term in context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the apparent meaning and the objection based on linguistic custom in a given fiqh debate: e.g., a debate about whether 'hand' in the Quran for tayammum includes the forearm. State the objection using 'urf al-lugha.
?
Construct a response using 'urf al-lugha: Suppose a claimant says 'the Prophet forbade eating meat after two days' and you object that 'meat' in the language includes chicken, but the claimant meant specifically red meat. How would you respond as a jadal respondent?

Objection by Implication: Al-Zahir bi al-Dalala
Author defines the third type of objection: disputing the apparent meaning via implication, with subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the two subtypes of objection by implication (taqdir mahdhuf and ibdal lafz makan lafz) in a juristic debate.

Core Idea
Implication objections require evidence to shift from literal to intended meaning.

In dialectical debates (jadal), an opponent may concede the apparent wording of a proof but argue that its true meaning is not the literal one. Instead, the intended meaning is derived through implication, requiring either a missing element to be supplied or a word to be substituted. This lesson unpacks two precise tools: taqdir mahdhuf and ibdal lafz makan lafz. Mastering them equips you to evaluate arguments in usul al-fiqh and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent meaning (zahir) being cited
Find the textual evidence the claimant is using. Notice its literal wording and what it seems to imply directly.
In the examples, the claimant cites hadith like 'فلا إذا' or 'فصاحب المتاع أحق بمتاعه'.
2
Determine the objector's claim about the meaning
Does the objector argue that an element is omitted (taqdir mahdhuf) or that a word should be replaced (ibdal lafz makan lafz)? Identify which subtype is being used.
Both examples in the passage use taqdir mahdhuf.
3
Assess whether there is contextual evidence for the implied meaning
The objector cannot arbitrarily add or substitute; there must be a dalil (evidence) that turns the speech from its literal sense to the intended interpretation.
The text explicitly states the implication requires 'ضرب من الدليل يصرف الكلام عن ظاهره'.
4
Evaluate the validity of the objection
Check if the implied meaning is plausible and supported by context. If the omission or substitution is not justified, the objection is weak.
In the fresh dates hadith, the omitted 'فلا يجوز إذا' is contextually reasonable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب – 3 – الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالظاهر بالدلالة : والظاهر بالدلالة هو ما لا يستدل له إلا بضرب من الدليل يصرف الكلام عن ظاهره إلى وجه يحتمله، وهو على نوعين: أحدهما يفتقر إلى تقدير محذوف، والآخر يفتقر إلى إبدال لفظ مكان لفظ . فأما الذي يفتقر إلى تقدير محذوف : فمثل أن يستدل المالكي في أنه لا يجوز بيع الرطب بالتمر بما روي عن النبي أنه سئل عن بيع الرطب بالتمر ، فقال : (أينقص الرطب إذا جف؟) فقيل له : نعم ، فقال : (فلا إذا) ، والتقدير فيه : فلا يجوز إذا . ومثاله كذلك : أن يستدل المالكي في أن البائع إذا وجد عين ماله عند المفلس رجع به، بقوله : (أيما رجل مات أو أفلس فصاحب المتاع أحق بمتاعه إذا وجده بعينه) ، فهذا تقديره : فالذي كان صاحب المتاع.
B-3: Objection to reasoning based on the apparent meaning by implication. The apparent meaning by implication is that which cannot be argued for except by a type of evidence that turns the speech from its apparent meaning to a possible interpretation. It is of two types: one requires supplying an omitted element, and the other requires substituting one word for another. As for that which requires an omitted element: such as the Maliki arguing that it is not permissible to sell fresh dates for dried dates, based on what was narrated from the Prophet that he was asked about selling fresh dates for dried dates, and he said: 'Do fresh dates decrease when they dry?' It was said: 'Yes', so he said: 'Then no', and the omitted element in it is: 'Then it is not permissible.' Another example: the Maliki arguing that if the seller finds his specific goods with the bankrupt, he takes them back, based on his saying: 'Any man who dies or becomes bankrupt, the owner of the goods has more right to his goods if he finds them specifically.' So its omitted element is: 'Then the one who was the owner of the goods [has more right].'
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of objection: disputing the apparent meaning by implication (al-dalala). The author explains that 'al-zahir bi al-dalala' is a meaning that is not apparent from the literal wording but requires evidence to turn the speech from its apparent sense to a plausible interpretation. This type is divided into two subtypes: one requiring an omitted element (taqdir mahdhuf) and one requiring a substitution of a word (ibdal lafz makan lafz). The author only provides examples for the first subtype. The first example is from Maliki fiqh: the Prophet's response 'Then no' (fala idhan) to the question about selling fresh dates for dried dates implies an omitted phrase: 'Then it is not permissible.' The second example is from a hadith about a bankrupt person: 'The owner of the goods has more right to his goods' implies an omitted phrase: 'Then the owner has more right.' In both cases, the objector claims that the apparent meaning alone is insufficient without supplying the omitted element.

Key Terms
الظاهر بالدلالة
تقدير محذوف
إبدال لفظ مكان لفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must be present for an objection by implication to be valid?
A) The opponent's interpretation must be arbitrary.
B) There must be evidence (dalil) that turns the speech from its apparent meaning.
C) The omitted element must be explicit in the text.
D) The objector must provide a completely different text.

Key Takeaway
Objection by implication (al-zahir bi al-dalala) requires a valid evidence to justify departing from the literal meaning of a text, either by supplying an omitted element or substituting a word.

The Hanafi Objection on the Scope of 'Ta'am' in Jadal
Author presents a Hanafi objection and Shafi'i response regarding the definition of 'food' in a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi objection and the Shafi'i response regarding the lexical definition of 'ta'am' in a jadal exchange, and analyze the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
In jadal, the definition of a single word can determine the outcome of an entire legal argument.

This passage presents a classic dialectical exchange in usul al-fiqh. The Hanafi argues that the term 'ta'am' (food) in a hadith should be interpreted broadly, while the Shafi'i defender counters by insisting that its lexical meaning is specifically wheat. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to see how linguistic arguments are used to narrow or expand the scope of a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
Read the Arabic and translation. The Hanafi claims 'ta'am' applies to all foods. The Shafi'i claims it is lexically restricted to wheat. Note the key evidence each uses.
The Hanafi uses the general usage; the Shafi'i appeals to a specific lexical definition.
2
Analyze the Hanafi objection
The Hanafi says there is nothing in the hadith to restrict the term to wheat. He relies on the apparent broad meaning. What type of argument is this?
This is a claim of semantic generality based on customary usage.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i response
The responder offers two arguments: (1) The lexical definition of 'ta'am' is wheat, even if the sense of being eaten is absent. (2) The hadith already includes other sustenances, leaving only wheat for 'ta'am'. How does each argument work?
The first is a linguistic claim; the second is a contextual elimination argument.
4
Evaluate the jadal move
Assess the strength of each position according to jadal rules. Does the Shafi'i's lexical claim have enough support? Does the Hanafi's reliance on general usage hold?
Consider that jadal allows appeals to language (lugha) and context (siyaq).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول الحنفي معترضا اسم الطعام واقع على كل ما يتطعم من تمر وسمن وغير ذلك، وليس في الحديث ما يدل على أن المراد به البر دون غيره. وجواب المستدل أن أن عرف هذه اللفظة اللغة القمح، وذلك أن يبين في اللغة يقول : إن ظاهر هذا اللفظ إذا أطلق يتناول البر، وإن كان معنى الطعم فيه غير موجود؛ ثم إن الحديث قد تضمن سائر أنواع الأقوات، فلم يبق ما يحمل عليه اسم الطعام إلا البر.
The Hanafi says, objecting: The word 'food' applies to everything that is eaten, such as dates, ghee, and other things, and there is nothing in the hadith indicating that it means wheat specifically. The responder's answer is that the lexical definition of this word is wheat, and that is by explaining in the language that the apparent meaning of this word when used unrestrictedly includes wheat, even if the meaning of 'being eaten' is not present in it; moreover, the hadith includes all other types of sustenance, so nothing remains that the name 'food' can be applied to except wheat.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. The Hanafi argues that the word 'ta'am' (food) in a hadith should be interpreted broadly, not restricted to wheat. The responder (likely a Shafi'i) counters by claiming that the lexical definition of 'ta'am' is specifically wheat, and that the apparent meaning when used absolutely is wheat, even if the sense of nourishment is absent. He further argues that since the hadith already includes other types of provisions, the only thing left that the word can apply to is wheat. This illustrates how disputants use linguistic definitions to narrow the scope of a term in argumentation.

الظاهر

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a term's definition can be narrowed or broadened to support a legal claim, and the disputant must provide linguistic or contextual evidence to justify the narrowing.

Arguments Requiring an Implicit Deleted Element
Author defines the first type of argument requiring an implicit element and gives two examples with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify arguments that rely on an implicit deleted element, formulate objections by proposing a different implicit, and defend the original argument using evidence.

Core Idea
An argument's apparent meaning may need an implicit element; the objector can counter by inserting a different implicit, but evidence must decide.

In jadal, many arguments from revealed texts require us to supply a missing word or phrase to complete the meaning. This lesson dissects the first type of such arguments, focusing on how the arguer establishes the implicit and how the objector can challenge it. The Maliki examples from fresh date sale and bankruptcy will ground the abstract method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument type
Recognize that the argument relies on 'apparent meaning by evidence' and specifically requires an implicit deleted element (taqdir mahdhuf). The arguer claims the speech is incomplete without supplying a word.
This is distinct from arguments that substitute a word.
2
Extract the arguer's implicit
In the fresh date hadith, the Maliki supplies 'la yajuz' (not permissible) after the Prophet's 'Fala idha'. Determine what implicit the arguer inserts to complete the meaning.
The implicit must be justified by evidence.
3
Formulate the objection
The objector supplies a different implicit, e.g., 'la ba's' (no harm). This changes the conclusion. Identify the alternative implicit and see how it leads to a different ruling.
The objection must also be plausible.
4
Analyze the response
The arguer refutes the objection with two arguments: (1) The answer must correspond to the question asked (the question was about permissibility, so the implicit must be about prohibition). (2) The Prophet's reasoning (decrease when dry) does not support the objector's implicit; if equality at contract were the cause, the Prophet would have mentioned equality.
Evaluate whether the response successfully defends the original implicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب – 3 – الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالظاهر بالدلالة : والظاهر بالدلالة هو ما لا يستدل له إلا بضرب من الدليل يصرف الكلام عن ظاهره إلى وجه يحتمله، وهو على نوعين: أحدهما يفتقر إلى تقدير محذوف، والآخر يفتقر إلى إبدال لفظ مكان لفظ .

ب - 3 - 1 - فأما الذي يفتقر إلى تقدير محذوف : فمثل أن يستدل المالكي في أنه لا يجوز بيع الرطب بالتمر بما روي عن النبي لا لا لا لا هو أنه سئل عن بيع الرطب بالتمر ، فقال : (أينقص الرطب إذا جف؟) فقيل له : نعم ، فقال : (فلا إذا) ، والتقدير فيه : فلا يجوز إذا .

ومثاله كذلك : أن يستدل المالكي في أن البائع إذا وجد عين ماله عند المفلس رجع به، بقوله : (أيما رجل مات أو أفلس فصاحب المتاع أحق بمتاعه إذا وجده بعينه) ، فهذا تقديره : فالذي كان صاحب المتاع، ويدل عليه بأنه لا يجوز أن يكون المراد به صاحب المتاع في الحال لأنه شرط فيه الإفلاس، وذلك لا يشترط في صاحب المتاع في الحال فيثبت أن المراد به الذي كان صاحب المتاع .

والاعتراض على هذا النوع من الاستدلال يقع من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يضمر السائل غير ما أضمره المستدل وذلك مثل أن يقول في الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالحديث الأول : إن المراد بقوله «فلا إذا» : فلا بأس إذا .

والجواب عنه من وجهين :

الأول : أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل : هل يجوز بيع الرطب بالتمر؟ فإذا قال : فلا إذا، وجب أن ينصرف الجواب إلى السؤال فيكون معناه : فلا يجوز إذا .

والجواب الثاني : أن هذا التعليل لا يصح على إضماركم، لأن كون الرطب مما ينقص في ثاني حال ليس بعلة في جواز بيعه لأنه لو لم ينقص أيضًا لكان أصح ، ولو أراد ما قلتموه لوجب أن يقول : أيتساويان حال العقد؟ لأن علة جواز البيع عندكم هي التساوي في حال العقد، ولمَّا عدل عنه إلى ما ذكرناه دل على بطلان قولهم .

والثاني : مثاله : أن يقول في مسألة الإفلاس ما أنكرت على من يقول : إن المراد بصاحب المتاع المشتري الذي هو صاحب له في الحال، وانما شرط الإفلاس حتى لا يظن ظان أنه لما أفلس صار البائع أحق به، والجواب عن ذلك ينصر المستدل الدليل الذي ذكره، ويسقط ما أورده المعترض من تأويل الدليل، بأن يقول : قوله ( أيما) من حروف الشرط، فالظاهر أن إفلاسه شرط في الاستحقاق، ويقول : لو كان ما قلتموه صحيحًا لقال : أيما رجل أفلس فهو أحق به ولم يقل : فصاحب المتاع، لأن ذكره قد تقدم .
B-3: Objection to argument by apparent meaning using evidence: Apparent meaning by evidence is that which is not deduced except by some kind of evidence that diverts the speech from its apparent meaning to a possible interpretation. It is of two types: one needs an implicit deleted element, and the other needs substitution of a word for a word.

B-3-1: As for that which needs an implicit deleted element: For example, the Maliki argues that it is not permissible to sell fresh dates for dried dates by what was narrated from the Prophet—he was asked about selling fresh dates for dried dates, and he said: 'Does the fresh date decrease when it dries?' It was said: Yes. He said: 'Then no, if so.' The implicit element is: 'Then it is not permissible if so.'

Another example: The Maliki argues that if the seller finds his property with a bankrupt person, he has priority, by his saying: 'Any man who dies or becomes bankrupt, the owner of the property has more right to his property if he finds it exactly as it was.' So the implicit element is: 'Then he who was the owner of the property.' And it indicates that it is not permissible for the intended meaning to be the current owner of the property because bankruptcy is a condition in it, and that is not a condition for the current owner, so it is established that the intended meaning is he who was the owner of the property.

Objection to this type of argument occurs in two ways: First: That the objector inserts an implicit element different from what the arguer inserted. For example, in objecting to the argument by the first hadith, he says: The intended meaning of his saying 'Then no, if so' is 'Then no harm if so.'

The answer to it is in two ways:
First: The Prophet was asked: Is it permissible to sell fresh dates for dried dates? When he said 'Then no, if so', the answer must correspond to the question, so its meaning is 'Then it is not permissible if so.'
Second answer: This reasoning is not valid according to your implicit element, because the fact that fresh dates decrease later is not a cause for the permissibility of selling it, because if it did not decrease as well, it would be more correct. If you meant what you claimed, he would have said: Do they equal at the time of contract? Because the cause of permissibility of sale according to you is equality at the time of contract. When he deviated to what we mentioned, it indicates the invalidity of your statement.

Second: Its example: He says in the bankruptcy case: What prevents you from saying that the intended meaning is the buyer who is the current owner? Bankruptcy is only mentioned so that no one thinks that when he becomes bankrupt, the seller becomes more entitled. The answer to that supports the arguer's evidence and invalidates the objector's interpretation, by saying: His word 'ayyuma' is a conditional particle, so the apparent meaning is that bankruptcy is a condition for entitlement. And he says: If what you said were correct, he would have said: 'Any man who becomes bankrupt, he is more entitled to it', and he would not have said 'the owner of the property', because mentioning it has preceded.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of a type of argument in jadal. The author defines 'apparent meaning by evidence' as a speech that requires some evidence to shift it from its apparent meaning to an acceptable interpretation. He then splits this into two types: one requiring an implicit deleted element (taqdir mahdhuf) and one requiring substitution of a word (ibdal lafz). For the first type, he gives two examples from Maliki jurisprudence. The first example is the famous hadith about selling fresh dates (ruthb) for dried dates (tamr). The Maliki argues that the Prophet's reply 'Fala idha' implies 'Fala yajuzu idha' (It is not permissible). The objection is that the objector may insert a different implicit, e.g., 'Fala ba'sa idha' (No harm). The author provides two answers: first, the answer must correspond to the question asked; second, the reasoning behind the Prophet's question (decrease in weight) is not a cause for permissibility, so the objector's implicit is invalid. The second example involves bankruptcy and the hadith about the seller's priority. The Maliki argues that the implicit is 'the one who was the owner' (not the current one). The objector claims it could be the current owner. The answer points out that the word 'ayyuma' (any) is conditional, so bankruptcy is a condition, which would not apply to the current owner. Thus the Malikis' implicit is correct.

Key Terms
الظاهر بالدلالة
تقدير محذوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) فلا يجوز إذا
C) فلا تكره إذا
D) فلا يصح إذا

Key Takeaway
When an argument relies on an implicit deleted element, the objector can challenge it by proposing a different implicit, but the arguer can defend using contextual and linguistic evidence.

Introduction to Argument by Word Substitution (Ibdal Lafz bi-Lafz)
Author introduces the second type of argument requiring substitution of a word for a word, with an incomplete example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify arguments that require word substitution (ibdal lafz makan lafz) and explain why the substitution is necessary for correct interpretation in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
In jadal, sometimes the literal words must be replaced to uncover the true argument.

In this passage, the author introduces a second type of argument by apparent meaning – one that cannot be completed without substituting a word for a word. This technique is crucial in usul al-fiqh and dialectic when a speaker's intended meaning is not captured by the literal expression. We'll explore al-Shafi'i's incomplete example to understand the method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim that requires substitution
Find the part of the speech that cannot be interpreted literally without replacement. In the example, al-Shafi'i argues against selling unseen items by description. The hadith he cites likely contains a word that must be replaced to yield the prohibition.
The example is incomplete in the source, so we infer the need for substitution.
2
Locate the word to be replaced
Determine which word in the evidence is the candidate for substitution. Often it is a general term that needs specification or a metaphorical expression that needs a literal equivalent.
Without the full hadith, we cannot specify exactly, but the principle remains.
3
Justify the substitution
Explain why the substitution is necessary: to preserve the intended meaning, to avoid contradiction with other texts, or to align with linguistic norms of the Sharia.
This step requires knowledge of usul al-fiqh principles like context (qarīna) and lexical consistency.

The Literal Meaning Defense in Dialectical Interpretation
Author presents an objection to a hadith-based argument and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two moves in a dialectical objection and response: word substitution and literal interpretation, using the hadith on selling what is not with you.

Core Idea
The first response keeps the wording literal; the objection required substitution.

In usul al-fiqh debates, a common tactic is to reinterpret a text's wording. Here, an objector claims the hadith 'Do not sell what is not with you' must be changed to 'what is not in your hand' to apply. The first response rejects this substitution, insisting the original wording is sufficient. This lesson walks you through the dialectical exchange and the principle of carrying words on their apparent meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector asserts that the hadith requires substituting 'with you' with 'in your hand' to be relevant. Find the exact statement in the Arabic where this substitution is demanded.
Look for 'لا يتم به إلا بإبدال لفظ مكان لفظ'.
2
Extract the first response's claim
The first response insists that the wording can be taken literally: 'what is not with you' means 'not in your possession'. No substitution is needed. Locate the part where this is explained.
The response cites the case of Hakim ibn Hizam to support the literal interpretation.
3
Compare the two interpretations
Notice the difference: the objector prefers a narrower meaning (physical hand), while the response maintains a broader legal meaning (possession). Which one better fits the context of the hadith as understood by the Prophet's companions?
This is a classic jadal move: showing the original wording already covers the case.
4
Assess the validity of the first response
The response argues that if the wording can be taken literally, it should not be shifted to another meaning without evidence. Is this a strong argument? Consider the principle of 'al-haqiqah fi al-lughah' (words are taken in their literal sense unless there is a reason otherwise).
This is a fundamental principle of usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال عليه الصلاة والسلام قال لحكيم بن حزام (لا تبع ما ليس عندك)، فهذا لا يتم به إلا بإبدال لفظ مكان لفظ، وذلك أن يقول : إن لفظ الحديث (لا) تبع ما ليس عندك والمراد به ما ليس بيدك، بدليل أنه يجوز أن يقال : هذا ملكي وليس عندي وهذا عندي وليس ملكي، وإذا دل ذلك على أن المراد به : لا تبع ما ليس بيدك، لم يجز بيع الغائب الموصوف . والطريق في الجواب عنه أمران : أحدهما : أن يبين أن اللفظ يحمل على مقتضاه ولا يحتاج إلى إبداله بإبدال لفظ آخر، وذلك أن يقول : إن معنى ما ليس عندك): ما ليس في ملكك، حكيم بن حزام كان سلعا يبيع في ملك غيره ثم يشتريها ويسلمها، فنهاه النبي عن ذلك، وإذا صح أن يحمل اللفظ على مقتضاه لم يجز نقله إلى معنى غيره إلا بدليل . والثاني : أن العلة عندكم في المنع من البيع ؛ عدم الرؤية، لا عدم كونها بيده، لأنه لو تقدمت الرؤية، جاز عندكم البيع وإن كانت السلعة غائبة، ولو كانت السلعة بيده ولم يرها المشتري لم يجز بيعه إياها، فلا يصح الاحتجاج بهذا الحديث.
The evidence: He, peace be upon him, said to Hakim ibn Hizam: 'Do not sell what is not with you.' This cannot be completed except by substituting one word for another. That is to say, the wording of the hadith is 'Do not sell what is not with you' but the intended meaning is 'what is not in your hand,' as evidence that it is permissible to say: 'This is my property but not with me' and 'This is with me but not my property.' When this indicates that the intended meaning is 'Do not sell what is not in your hand,' then selling an absent described good is not permissible. The way to answer it is two matters: first, to show that the wording is taken according to its implication and does not need to be substituted with another wording. That is to say, the meaning of 'what is not with you' is 'what is not in your possession.' Hakim ibn Hizam had goods he sold that were in the possession of others, then he would buy them and deliver them. The Prophet prohibited him from that. If it is correct to take the wording according to its implication, then it is not permissible to transfer it to another meaning except with evidence. Second, the effective cause according to you in prohibiting the sale is lack of sight, not lack of it being in his hand, because if sight had preceded, the sale would be permissible according to you even if the goods are absent. And if the goods were in his hand but the buyer did not see them, the sale would not be permissible. So the argument with this hadith is not valid.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical analysis of an objection to using the hadith 'Do not sell what is not with you' as evidence against selling absent goods. The objector argues that the hadith requires substituting 'with you' with 'in your hand' to correctly apply to the case. The author then provides two responses. The first response insists on the literal meaning: 'with you' means 'in your possession', and the hadith is correctly applied because Hakim ibn Hizam was selling goods not in his possession. The second response shifts attention to the underlying cause ('illa): the prohibition is due to lack of sight, not lack of possession. This is shown by examples where sight precedes (sale allowed) versus goods in hand but unseen (sale not allowed). Thus the hadith does not support the opponent's claim. The argument is juristic, using principles of usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الاستدلال (al-istidlāl): The process of inference or argumentation using textual evidence, here specifically the hadith. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
العلة (al-ʿilla): The effective cause or rationale behind a legal ruling; here it is identified as lack of sight. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, 'illa is a key term for analogical reasoning; here used in a jadal context.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first response, what is the correct meaning of 'ما ليس عندك' in the hadith?
A) What is not in your hand physically
B) What is not in your possession (ownership)
C) What is not described to you
D) What is not present in the market

Key Takeaway
The first response shows that adhering to the literal meaning of a text can block an objection that demands substitution, provided the literal meaning fits the context.

The Role of ʿilla in Dialectical Refutation
Author presents an objection to a hadith-based argument and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how shifting the debate to the effective cause (ʿilla) refutes an objection, using the second response from the hadith of selling what is not with you.

Core Idea
The second response changes the ground: the prohibition is about seeing, not possession.

After the first response defends the literal meaning, the second response takes a different tack: it examines the underlying cause (ʿilla) of the prohibition. The objector assumed the hadith prohibits selling absent goods because they are not physically present. But the author argues that the real cause is lack of sight (ru'ya), not lack of physical possession. This move undercuts the objection entirely. This lesson focuses on that argument and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second response's claim
The second response states that the effective cause (ʿilla) for prohibiting the sale is lack of sight, not lack of the good being in hand. Find the sentence that explicitly says this.
Key phrase: 'العلة عندكم في المنع من البيع؛ عدم الرؤية'.
2
Understand the supporting examples
Two examples are given: (1) if sight precedes the contract, the sale is allowed even if the good is absent; (2) if the good is in hand but unseen, the sale is not allowed. These show that the ruling depends on sight, not possession.
This is a standard usul al-fiqh argument: testing the ʿilla by counterexamples.
3
Connect to the hadith
The hadith 'Do not sell what is not with you' is about possession, not sight. Therefore, the hadith is irrelevant if the real ʿilla is sight. The objector's argument thus fails because it misidentifies the cause.
This is a powerful refutation: showing that the evidence (hadith) does not address the actual legal cause.
4
Evaluate the strength of the refutation
Consider whether the objector could respond by arguing that lack of sight is included in 'not with you'? The author anticipates that, but the examples show a clear distinction: something can be 'with you' (in hand) yet unseen, and vice versa. The second response seems conclusive within the jadal framework.
This move is a classic 'naqd al-ʿilla' (critique of the cause) in usul al-fiqh.

Objection: General Term Does Not Cover the Disputed Point
Author explains the first type of objection to general proof with an example from tayammum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of an objection to a general proof (that the general term does not include the point of disagreement) and evaluate the response, using the tayammum example.

Core Idea
A general proof can be countered by denying that the general term applies to the specific case.

In this lesson, we examine a classic dialectical move: the objector claims that the general term used in a proof does not actually include the specific point under dispute. The example comes from a dispute between a Maliki and a Shafi'i over the validity of tayammum with pebbles. The Maliki relies on the general word 'sa'id' in the hadith, while the Shafi'i restricts its meaning. We will walk through the structured exchange and the author's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general proof being used
The Maliki cites the hadith: 'إن الصعيد الطيب وضوء المسلم' as a general proof for tayammum with any earth. The word 'الصعيد' is the general term.
The general proof is a textual proof ('umum) that the Maliki argues applies to all types of earth.
2
Locate the objector's challenge
The Shafi'i objects: 'لا أسلم أن الصعيد واقع على الحجارة' - he denies that 'sa'id' includes stones. This is an objection that the general term does not cover the point of disagreement (pebbles).
The objector does not deny the hadith's authenticity but restricts its scope.
3
Analyze the objector's evidence
The Shafi'i cites Ibn Abbas: 'الصعيد التراب' to argue that 'sa'id' means only dust. Consider the strength of this evidence: it is a statement of a Companion, but is it linguistic or interpretive?
The objector uses a specific report to support his restriction.
4
Examine the author's rebuttal
The author responds by citing linguistic consensus: linguists say 'sa'id' is the face of the earth, which includes stones. He also questions the authenticity of Ibn Abbas's statement and clarifies that even if true, it only states predominance, not exclusivity.
The rebuttal shifts the basis from a Companion's statement to established linguistic authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - 1 - الاعتراض على العموم بأنه لا يتناول موضع الخلاف : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على جواز التيمم بالحصى، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : ( إن الصعيد الطيب وضوء المسلم، ولو لم يجد الماء عشر سنين)، ولم يخص حجارة من غيرها، فوجب حمل ذلك على كل ما وقع عليه اسم الصعيد من حجارة أو غيرها إلا ما خصه الدليل فيقول الشافعي معترضا : لا أسلم أن الصعيد واقع على الحجارة، وإنما يتناول التراب فقط، ولذلك قال ابن عباس : الصعيد التراب . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض: أن هذا غلط، لأن أهل اللغة ذكروا أن الصعيد وجه الأرض، وقد قال أبو إسحاق الزجاج : لا أعلم في ذلك خلافا في أهل اللغة ؛ وقول ابن عباس غير محفوظ عنه، ولو ثبت لكان معناه أن معظم الأرض التراب ؛ ثم إن قول ابن عباس الصعيد التراب»، ليس فيه دليل على أن غير التراب لا يسمى صعيدا، وإنما فيه إثبات هذا الاسم للتراب فقط.
C-1- Objection to the general [proof] that it does not include the point of disagreement: Example: The Maliki argues for the permissibility of tayammum with pebbles based on what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Indeed the good earth is the ablution of a Muslim, even if he does not find water for ten years.' He did not specify stones from others, so it is necessary to apply it to everything that the name 'sa'id' falls upon, whether stones or others, except what evidence specifies. Then the Shafi'i objects by saying: I do not accept that 'sa'id' applies to stones, but only to dust. Therefore Ibn Abbas said: 'Sa'id is dust.' The answer to this objection: This is a mistake, because the linguists mentioned that 'sa'id' is the face of the earth. Abu Ishaq al-Zajjaj said: I do not know any disagreement among linguists on this. The statement of Ibn Abbas is not preserved from him. Even if it were established, its meaning would be that most of the earth is dust. Then indeed the statement of Ibn Abbas 'Sa'id is dust' is not evidence that other than dust is not called 'sa'id'; rather it only affirms this name for dust.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage explains the first type of objection to a general proof: the objector claims that the general term does not include the specific point of disagreement. The example involves tayammum with pebbles: the Maliki uses the hadith 'the good earth is the ablution of a Muslim' to argue that any 'sa'id' (including stones) is valid. The Shafi'i objects by denying that 'sa'id' includes stones, citing Ibn Abbas that it means only dust. The author then refutes this objection by citing linguistic consensus that 'sa'id' means the face of the earth, which includes stones. Ibn Abbas's statement is either unproven or merely stating that dust is most common, not exclusive. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الصعيد
الاعتراض
العموم
موضع الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On what basis does the author refute the Shafi'i objection regarding the word 'sa'id'?
A) The hadith is weak.
B) Linguists agree that 'sa'id' means the face of the earth.
C) Ibn Abbas's statement is the definitive proof.
D) The Maliki has the stronger argument.

Key Takeaway
An objection that a general term does not include the disputed point can be refuted by demonstrating the term's broader linguistic meaning.
